Refuting Shi'a Claims on Uthman's Imamate
Baqillani argues that Shi'a criticisms of Uthman also undermine Ali's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani exposes an inconsistency in Shi'a arguments about Uthman's imamate by applying their own standards to Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
If Shi'a reject human choice in Ali's imamate, they cannot use it against Uthman.

Baqillani begins by affirming the validity of both Uthman's and Ali's imamates. He then turns the tables on the Shi'a, who criticized Uthman's imamate based on the actions of Abd al-Rahman and rebels. He points out that according to their own doctrine of nass, such human actions are irrelevant, so they cannot criticize Uthman without also undermining Ali.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice how Baqillani structures his refutation.
2
Identify the Shi'a premise
The Shi'a claim that imamate is established by nass (divine text) and therefore human actions like withdrawal do not affect it. Baqillani uses this premise against them.
This is key to the refutation.
3
Follow the logical consequences
Baqillani shows that if the Shi'a insist on nass, they cannot critique Uthman's imamate based on human actions; if they switch to the ikhtiyar argument, that also fails and backfires.
How does Baqillani conclude that the Shi'a argument is self-defeating?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نبرأ إلى الله تعالى من القدح في إمامتهما جميعاً غير أن الشيعة تفتح على أنفسها من هذا الباب ما لا قبل لهم بدفعه. وليس تفسد إمامة عليّ بخلع من عقدها له ولا بالتأويل عليها بأنها عُقدت على شرط فيها ولا يوهنها قعود من قعد عنها فكذلك لا تبطل إمامة عثمان بما حكوه عن عبد الرحمن ولا بسعي أوغاد وأهل فتنة إليه وتعديهم عليه لأن إمامته قد ثبتت وصحت فلا يقدح فيها شيء مما ذكروه . فإن قالت الشيعة : نحن لا نعتبر بقول طلحة والزبير وخلعهما وقعودهما لأن طريق الإمامة النص من النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم قيل لهم : فليس يجب أن ينزلوا معنا إلى الكلام في إمامة عثمان وعقد عبد الرحمن وخلعه لأن الخوض فيها عندكم منكر فإن قالوا : إنما نسألكم ويُلزمكم على مذهب أهل الاختيار، قيل لهم : قد أبنا لكم أن ذلك لا يلزم على أصولنا وأنه لو لزم لـعــاد بإبطال إمامة عليّ ؛ وذلك فاسد.
We absolve ourselves before Allah Almighty from criticizing the imamate of both. However, the Shi'a open for themselves a door that they cannot repel. The imamate of Ali is not invalidated by the withdrawal of those who contracted it for him, nor by interpretation that it was contracted on a condition, nor is it weakened by the sitting out of those who sat out. Likewise, the imamate of Uthman is not invalidated by what they narrated from Abd al-Rahman, nor by the efforts of vile people and people of fitna against him and their transgression upon him, because his imamate has been established and confirmed, so nothing of what they mentioned can detract from it. If the Shi'a say: 'We do not consider the words of Talha and al-Zubayr and their withdrawal and sitting out, because the way of imamate is the text (nass) from the Prophet (peace be upon him)', it is said to them: 'Then it is not necessary for them to descend with us into discussion about the imamate of Uthman and the contract of Abd al-Rahman and its dissolution, because delving into that is objectionable (munkar) in your view.' If they say: 'We are only questioning you and imposing the argument upon you according to the school of choice (al-ikhtiyar)', it is said to them: 'We have already clarified to you that this does not follow from our principles, and if it did follow, it would return to invalidate the imamate of Ali; and that is corrupt.'
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative refutation of the Shi'a position regarding the imamate of Uthman. Baqillani begins by stating that he absolves himself from criticizing the imamate of either Ali or Uthman, but then points out an inconsistency in the Shi'a argument. The Shi'a had claimed that the opposition of Talha and al-Zubayr to Ali's caliphate did not affect its validity because the imamate is established by nass (divine designation). Baqillani retorts that if that is the case, then the Shi'a should not even discuss Uthman's imamate based on the actions of Abd al-Rahman and the rebels, because according to them, imamate is not established by human choice. If the Shi'a then shift their ground and say they are arguing according to the Sunni school of ikhtiyar (election), Baqillani responds that even on that basis, their argument does not hold, and if it did, it would also invalidate Ali's imamate, which they reject. Thus he exposes the self-defeating nature of their position.

Key Terms
إمامة
نص
اختيار
خلع
بيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani is criticizing both Uthman and Ali.
Correction: Baqillani affirms the imamate of both and uses Shi'a premises to refute their critique of Uthman.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani respond to the Shi'a claim that Talha and al-Zubayr's opposition does not affect Ali's imamate because it is based on nass?
A) He agrees with them.
B) He says that if that is the case, then the imamate of Uthman cannot be challenged based on human actions either, so the Shi'a should not discuss it.
C) He argues that the imamate of Uthman is also based on nass.
D) He says Talha and al-Zubayr were wrong to oppose.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's refutation shows that the Shi'a argument against Uthman's imamate is self-defeating because it would also invalidate Ali's imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, have you ever held a standard for others that you refuse to apply to yourself? Baqillani exposes the Shi'a doing exactly that. Would you be willing to let someone point out such an inconsistency in your own reasoning—or do you, like the Shi'a, shift your ground to protect your position? Commit today to inviting a trusted friend to point out one inconsistency in your beliefs.
?
This lesson shows that arguments built on double standards collapse under their own weight. Is there a belief or practice you defend that, if turned around, would prove the opposite? Imagine someone uses your own logic against you—would it stand? Before you finish today, write down one such belief and ask yourself: 'Am I willing to abandon it if it is self-defeating?'

Uthman Was Killed Unjustly: The Position and Proof
Author states that Uthman was killed unjustly and gives the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni theological position that Uthman was killed unjustly and the proof from his virtues and legitimate imamate.

Core Idea
Uthman's killing was unjust because he had no fault and his killers had no valid evidence.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani establishes the orthodox Sunni position regarding the death of the third caliph, Uthman. He directly addresses the question of whether Uthman deserved death or was unjustly killed, and then provides a proof based on Uthman's well-established virtues and the legitimacy of his imamate. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Let the author's own rhythm and structure come through.
Notice the question-and-answer format.
2
Identify the key claims
Find the three main assertions: Uthman was killed unjustly, he had no fault warranting death or removal, and the killers had no valid excuse.
3
Examine the proof
The author points to Uthman's established faith, purity, precedence, virtue, jihad, valid imamate, and mandatory obedience. Understand why these make the proof self-evident.
The author says repetition of these virtues is unnecessary.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author emphasizes that the killers had not even a specious argument. This teaches the necessity of evidence in matters of blood and leadership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في مقتل عثمان رضي والدليل على أنه قتل مظلوماً
الله عنه
فإن قال قائل : فهل تقولون إن عثمان قتل مستحقاً للقتل أم مظلوماً وهل كان منه حَدَث أوجب قتله والمطالبة بخلعه أم لا؟ قيل له : نقول إن عثمان رضي الله عنه قتل مظلوماً وإنه لم يكن منه ما يوجب قتله ولا المطالبة بخلعـه ولا سقوط عدالته وموالاته وإن الذين تولوا قتله والإغراق في السعي عليه أهلُ فتنة ولفيف الأمصار ومن لا مدخل له في هذا الشأن أعني أمر الإمامة وحلهـا وعقدها وإنهم لم يستندوا في شيء مما خرجوا إليه في أمره إلى ما يمكن أن يكون شبهة فضلا أن يكون حجة. فإن قالوا وما الدليل على ذلك؟ عن الدليل عليه أنه قد ثبت من إيمان عثمان ونزاهته وسابقته وفضله وجهاده ما من فضائله ومن صحة إمامته وثبوت بيعته ووجوب طاعته والانقياد له ما نستغني عن إعادته وردّ قول فيه وإذا كان ذلك كذلك أن نعتقد أنه على هذه الأوصاف وأنه قتل مظلوماً حتى يذكر القاتلون لـه والساعون عليه جميع والمنتصرون لفجورهم ما يبيحون به دمه على ذلك الوجه ويوجب خلع طاعته وزوال عدالته ؛ وأنّى لهم بذلك؟
Chapter on the discussion of the killing of Uthman, may God be pleased with him, and the proof that he was killed unjustly. If a questioner says: Do you say that Uthman was killed deserving execution or unjustly? And was there an event from him that necessitated his killing and the demand to remove him or not? It is said to him: We say that Uthman, may God be pleased with him, was killed unjustly, and that there was not from him anything that necessitated his killing, nor the demand to remove him, nor the falling of his reliability and allegiance. And that those who undertook his killing and the extremism in striving against him are people of fitna and a mixed rabble of the cities, and those who have no involvement in this matter, I mean the matter of the imamate and its appointment and termination. And that they did not rely in anything of what they went out to in his affair on anything that could be a specious argument, let alone be a proof. Then if they say: And what is the proof of that? The proof is that it has been established from the faith of Uthman, his purity, his precedence, his virtue, and his jihad—among his virtues—and from the validity of his imamate, the establishment of his oath of allegiance, the obligation of obedience to him and yielding to him, what we are independent of repeating and refuting any statement about it. And since that is the case, it is necessary that we believe that he was upon these attributes and that he was killed unjustly, until the killers and those who strive against him and those who support their immorality mention what would permit his blood in that manner and necessitate removing obedience to him and the removal of his reliability. And how could they have that?
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This is a combined statement-of-position and proof passage. The author begins with the chapter heading, then presents a hypothetical objection: was Uthman deserving of execution or unjustly killed? The answer firmly states he was unjustly killed, with no cause for killing, removal, or loss of reliability. The author describes the killers as fitna-mongers and a motley rabble with no standing in the imamate, lacking even a specious argument. When the proof is requested, the author points to Uthman's well-known faith, purity, precedence, virtue, jihad, valid imamate, and mandatory obedience—so clear that repetition is unnecessary. Therefore, we must believe he was upon these attributes until the killers produce evidence permitting his blood—which they cannot.

Key Terms
مظلوماً
مستحقاً للقتل
الإمامة
عدالته
شبهة
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that political expediency justifies killing a ruler if it brings stability.
Correction: The author guards against this by emphasizing Uthman's virtue and the lack of any valid excuse from the killers, showing that killing a righteous leader without evidence is unjust.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can't the killers claim any justification for Uthman's death?
A) Because Uthman was a caliph
B) Because Uthman had no fault
C) Because they had no proof, not even a specious argument
D) Because the killers were not qualified

Key Takeaway
Uthman was killed unjustly, and the burden of proof lies with those who claim otherwise; since his virtues and legitimate imamate are established, his killers had no valid justification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone you respect is accused of wrongdoing, do you instinctively distance yourself or do you demand evidence before changing your opinion? How does Uthman's situation challenge you to be more just in your assessments?
?
Are there people in your life—family, friends, leaders—whose good qualities you know well but whom you have doubted because of rumors? What would it cost you to defend them as the author defends Uthman?
?
Have you ever joined a group that was 'certain' about someone's guilt without real proof? How can you ensure that you never become part of the 'rabble' that condemns without evidence?

Even if Removal Was Deserved, Killing Was Not Permitted
Author argues that killing Uthman was forbidden even if he deserved removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic legal distinction between a ruler who deserves removal and the impermissibility of killing him without just cause.

Core Idea
Deserving removal does not justify killing; only lawful procedures apply.

This passage from Imam Baqillani addresses a critical point of Sunni political theology: even if one concedes that a ruler like Uthman had done something warranting removal, the rebels' method—killing him—was categorically forbidden. Baqillani grounds this in the ruler's lack of armed resistance, showing that the proper response is restraint and lawful procedure, not vigilante violence. As you read, notice how the author builds his argument on the absence of combatant status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Let the author's own words establish the argument before you analyze it.
The phrase 'لم يخم داراً' is uncertain but contextually means he did not fortify himself.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Notice that the author begins with 'even if' (وعلى أنه لو). This concedes a premise for the sake of argument. What is being conceded? What is the conclusion?
The author concedes that Uthman may have deserved removal, but the conclusion is that killing was still not permitted.
3
Understand the reason: no war, no combatant status
The author lists actions Uthman did not do: fortify a house, defy the community, set up war. Why are these relevant? Consider the legal category of a combatant (muharib) in Islamic law.
Without these, the rebels could not claim they were fighting a legitimate war against an unjust ruler.
4
List the permissible alternatives
The author enumerates what should have been done: arrest, imprisonment, exile, or coercing abdication. Reflect on why these are lawful and killing is not.
These alternatives show that even a deposed ruler retains some rights and protections.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you have grasped the central point of the passage.
The question focuses on the proper actions according to Baqillani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أنه لو ثبت عليه أمر يستحق به خلع الطاعة ويجب به زوال العدالة لم يكن مبيحاً لقتله على ذلك الوجه لأنه لم يخم داراً ويمتنع على المسلمين ولا نَصَبَ الحرب بينه وبين من سار إليه فقد كان يجب عليهم القبض عليه لما أخذوه وتمكنوا من داره وحريمه أو حبسه وإبعاده عن المدينة أو أخذه بغاية الإرهاب بخلع نفسه لو كان مستحقاً للخلع فأما أن يُقتل على ذلك الوجه وهو غير ناصب للحرب فضلال وظلم لا محالة
And that even if an affair is established upon him that deserves the removal of obedience and necessitates the removal of reliability, it would not permit his killing in that manner, because he did not fortify a house and become defiant against the Muslims, nor did he set up war between him and those who marched against him. So it would have been obligatory upon them to arrest him when they seized him and gained control of his house and his sanctuary, or to imprison him and expel him from Medina, or to take him by extreme intimidation to abdicate himself if he deserved removal. As for killing him in that manner while he is not setting up war, it is error and injustice without doubt.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This is a secondary argument: even if one concedes that Uthman had done something warranting removal, the rebels' method—killing him—was impermissible. The author gives the reason: Uthman did not fortify himself or wage war. He was not in a state of armed rebellion. Therefore, the proper actions would have been arrest, imprisonment, exile, or coercive abdication. Since the rebels killed him without him being a combatant, their act is unequivocal error and injustice.

Key Terms
خلع الطاعة
مبيح
ناصب للحرب
فضلال وظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that a ruler who deserves removal can be killed by the community.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that killing is only permissible if the ruler is a combatant (nāsib li-l-ḥarb). Otherwise, lawful actions are arrest, imprisonment, exile, or forced abdication.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the proper action for the rebels if Uthman deserved removal?
A) Kill him immediately
B) Arrest, imprison, exile, or force abdication
C) Leave him alone and ignore his faults
D) Declare him an apostate

Key Takeaway
Even a ruler who deserves removal cannot be killed unless he is a combatant; only lawful alternatives like arrest or exile are permitted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you felt justified in harming someone because they 'deserved' it? How does this passage challenge the feeling that punishment can be taken into your own hands?
?
What would it look like for you to apply the principle of lawful procedures—arrest, imprisonment, or exile—when you encounter injustice in your own relationships or community, rather than retaliating?
?
Are there people in your life you have mentally 'convicted' without giving them due process? What concrete step will you take today to treat them with the fairness this passage demands?

The Rebels Were Not Qualified to Punish Uthman
Author argues that the rebels lacked authority to execute any punishment on Uthman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rebels lacked the authority to punish Uthman even if he deserved it, and why they are considered sinful.

Core Idea
Only qualified peers and legitimate authorities may punish a ruler, not the common subjects.

Imam Baqillani presents a key argument in defense of Uthman: even if the caliph had deserved execution or removal, the rebels were not the right people to do it. He establishes that only those of equal rank and with legitimate involvement in the imamate can undertake such actions. This passage clarifies a fundamental principle of Islamic political theology: authority to punish must be properly delegated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow: even if Uthman deserved punishment, the rebels were not qualified.
2
Identify the author's main premise
The author concedes hypothetically that Uthman might have deserved killing, removal, or exile. But he immediately rejects the rebels' authority to do so. Find the conditional statement (لو استحق) and the conclusion (لم يجز).
3
Understand the criteria for authority
The author lists reasons: the rebels are not of Uthman's peers (أضرابه), not his equals, not close in rank, and have no involvement in the imamate or in appointing/deposing leaders. Only such peers (أكفاؤه) and those with legitimate authority could act.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the common subjects (الرعية) have no right to take blood or enforce penalties even on the lowest person, so how much less on Uthman? Therefore, the rebels must be declared sinful (تفسيقهم) for killing him, regardless of his desert.
This is a strong argument that the ends do not justify the means without proper authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه لو استحق قتله وخلعه وإبعاده لم يجز أن يتولى ذلك من أمره الذين ساروا إليه لأنهم ليسوا من أضرابه ولا أشكاله وممن يداني منزلته ولا ممن له مدخل في الإمامة ولا في عقدها وحلها والاعتراض على أهلها وإنما يعتد بمثل ما جروا إليه ويكون لأحد فيه أدنى تعلق لو تولى ذلك منه أكفاؤه ومن له مدخل في هذا الشأن وليس للرعية كافة أن يتولوا دَمَ من هو دون عثمان ولا إقامة أقل الحدود على أقل الناس قدراً فكيف بعثمان مع ما ذكرناه من أحواله؟ وهذا واضح في أن القوم يجب تفسيقهم بقتله لو كان ممن يستحق القتل فضلاً عن أن يكون غير مستحق له
And that even if he deserved his killing, removal, and exile, it would not be permissible for those who marched against him to undertake that in his affair, because they are not of his peers nor his equals, nor of those who approach his rank, nor of those who have involvement in the imamate, nor in its establishment and dissolution, nor in objecting to its people. Rather, such an action is only considered from his peers and those who have involvement in this matter. And it is not for the common subjects to undertake the blood of one who is beneath Uthman, nor to establish the least of the prescribed punishments on the least of people in status. So how about Uthman, given what we have mentioned of his qualities? And this is clear in that the people must be declared sinful for killing him, even if he were one who deserved execution, let alone that he does not deserve it.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This is an argument about the qualifications of those carrying out any penalty. The author states that even if Uthman had deserved execution, removal, or exile, the rebels were not the appropriate persons to enact it. They were not his peers in rank, nor did they have any role in the imamate or in appointing or deposing leaders. Only Uthman's peers (أكفاؤه) and those with legitimate authority could have undertaken such measures. The common subjects (الرعية) do not have the right to take blood or enforce even the least penalty on the lowest person, so how much less for Uthman? Therefore, the rebels must be declared sinful (تفسيقهم) for killing him, regardless of his desert.

Key Terms
أضرابه
أكفاؤه
تفسيقهم
الرعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anyone can punish a corrupt ruler.
Correction: Only those with specific authority and standing, such as peers and those involved in the imamate, may undertake such actions. The common subjects have no such right.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the rebels were not qualified to kill Uthman even if he deserved it?
A) Because they did not have a judge
B) Because they were not his peers nor had authority in the imamate
C) Because he was still the caliph
D) Because they were outnumbered

Key Takeaway
The rebels are sinful for killing Uthman because they lacked the authority to do so, regardless of whether he deserved it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see wrongdoing in your community, do you first check your own qualifications and standing before taking action? How does this lesson challenge your impulse to act on your own authority?
?
What does this teaching reveal about your assumptions regarding justice? Are you willing to trust that only those with proper standing can correct a wrong, even if it means waiting or doing nothing?
?
If you were in a position of authority, would you ensure that your actions against a wrongdoer are carried out with the proper legitimacy, or would you rely on your own judgment? How can you apply this lesson to your daily life?

Motives and Leaders of the Rebels Against Uthman
Author lists rebels' motives and leaders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the personal and political motives behind the rebellion against Uthman and who the key leaders were.

Core Idea
The rebellion was driven by personal grudges, not religious reform.

In this passage, the author identifies the real reasons behind the rebellion against Caliph Uthman. Contrary to later sectarian narratives, he emphasizes that the rebels were motivated by worldly ambitions and personal grievances. He also names the leaders who orchestrated the uprising.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's use of 'أحقاد' (grudges) as the overarching motive.
2
Identify the motives
The passage lists several reasons: seeking authority, anger at governors, childhood grievances, denied requests. Note each one and consider how they are all personal rather than religious.
3
Note the leaders named
The author gives a list of leaders from Egypt, Basra, and Kufa. Write down their names and affiliations. This shows the rebellion had a coordinated leadership.
The names include al-Ghafiqi, Kinana, Sudan, and others.
4
Reflect on the historical context
Ask yourself why the author is so explicit about the personal nature of the revolt. What does this tell us about the sectarian debates of his time?
This connects to later controversies about Uthman's caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكرنا في غير هذا الكتاب أسباب هؤلاء القوم الذين عَدَوا عليه وكل واحد منهم والذي بعثهم على السير إليه وأنها كانت أحقاداً عليه لأجل إمْرَةٍ طلبوها ولأجل غيظ منهم على أمرائه ولأن بعضهم كان طفلاً في حَجْرِه ولأن بعضهم حرمه بعض طلبته إلى غير ذلك مما لا حاجة بنا إلى ذكره . وقادة هؤلاء القوم الذين أشاروا عليهم وحملوهم على ما فعلوه الغافقي المصري إمام القوم، وكنانة بن بشر التجيبي، وسودان بن حمران، بن بدیل بن ورقاء الخُزاعي، ومن قادة البصريين : حكم بن جبلة العبدي فيمن صحبه منهم ، ومن أهل الكوفة : ملك بن الحارث الأشتر النخعي في رجال قد سميناهم(۱). وقد كان هؤلاء أثاروا الفتنة مُدة قبل قتل عثمان رضي الله عنه ورأى من الرأي إبعادهم عن المدينة فأخرج منهم عن المدينة سبعة عشر نفساً ، منهم القوم الذين سميناهم ومنهم على ما ذكر صعصعة بن صوجان وزید بن صوجان العبديان وعبد الله بن الكواء، وعمرو بن الحمق، في آخرين.. فكان معاوية يقربهم ويدنيهم ويحضرهم طعامه ويُكثر إذكارهم بالله ويخوفهم شق العصا والفتك بإمام الأمة وتعظيم حرمة الإمامة ووجوب لزوم الجماعة إلى أن قال له زيد بن صوجان يوماً: «كم تكثر علينا بالإمْرَةِ ،وبقريش فوالله ما زالت العرب تأكل من قوائم سيوفها وقريش تأكل من متاجرها ؛ فقال له معاوية: «اسكت لا أم لك أذكرك بالإسلام وتذكرني بالجاهلية؟ قبح الله من كثر على أمير المؤمنين بكم،
And we have mentioned in other than this book the reasons of these people who transgressed against him, each one of them, and what sent them to march against him—that they were grudges against him on account of a position of authority that they sought, and on account of anger at his governors, and because some of them had been children in his care, and because some of them were denied some of their requests, and other things which we need not mention. The leaders of these people who advised them and incited them to do what they did were: al-Ghafiqi al-Misri, the imam of the people; Kinana bin Bishr al-Tujibi; Sudan bin Hamran; [and] bin Badil bin Warqa' al-Khuza'i; and from the leaders of the Basrans: Hukm bin Jabala al-Abdi, along with those who accompanied him; and from the people of Kufa: Malik bin al-Harith al-Ashtar al-Nakha'i, along with men we have named. And these people had stirred up fitna for a period before the killing of Uthman, may God be pleased with him. And it was deemed wise to expel them from Medina, so he expelled from them from Medina seventeen souls, including the people we have named, and among them as mentioned: Sa'sa'a bin Sawjan and Zayd bin Sawjan al-Abdiyan; and Abdullah bin al-Kawwa'; and Amr bin al-Hamiq, among others. And Muawiyah used to bring them close and honor them and invite them to his food and frequently remind them of God and warn them against splitting the staff and killing the imam of the ummah, and the sanctity of the imamate, and the obligation of adhering to the community, until Zayd bin Sawjan said to him one day: 'How often do you remind us of authority and of Quraysh? By God, the Arabs have always eaten from the edges of their swords while Quraysh eat from their trades.' So Muawiyah said: 'Be silent, may you have no mother! I remind you of Islam and you remind me of Jahiliyyah? May God disgrace the one who frequently reminds the Commander of the Faithful of you.'
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage provides historical details: the author states that the rebels' motives were personal—grudges for power, anger at Uthman's governors, childhood grievances, and denied requests. He then names the leaders: the Egyptian al-Ghafiqi, Kinana, Sudan, bin Badil, the Basran Hukm bin Jabala, and the Kufan Malik al-Ashtar. These men had been stirring fitna for some time, and Uthman expelled seventeen of them from Medina. The author then recounts an anecdote about Muawiyah hosting these rebels and advising them against rebellion, warning them of the sanctity of the imamate. Zayd bin Sawjan replied with a critique of Quraysh, to which Muawiyah responded harshly, condemning the return to jahiliyyah.

Key Terms
أحقاد
إمرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rebels were motivated by religious zeal to correct Uthman's innovations.
Correction: The author explicitly says their motives were personal grudges (أحقاد) and worldly ambitions, not religious reform.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what was the primary motivation for the rebels against Uthman?
A) Religious zeal
B) Personal grudges and desire for power
C) Uthman's religious innovations
D) Foreign influence

Key Takeaway
The rebellion against Uthman was fueled by personal grudges and political ambitions, not religious zeal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever allowed a personal grudge against an authority figure to silently justify disobedience or criticism in your heart? This lesson shows that even 'religious' movements can stem from unaddressed pride. Who do you need to forgive today to protect your soul from rebellion?
?
The rebels used the worldly excuse of 'anger at governors' to march against the rightful caliph. In what areas of your life do you disguise selfish ambition as righteous anger? Will you ask Allah to purify your intentions even when you feel wronged?
?
When you disagree with a leader, do you seek correction through proper Islamic channels, or do you find yourself gathering allies to oppose them? This passage warns that rebellion often begins with small grudges. What step will you take today to submit to authority for the sake of unity?

Muawiyah's Warning and Zayd's Reply
Anecdote of Muawiyah and the rebels' exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Muawiyah's attempt to dissuade the rebels and the revealing exchange with Zayd bin Sawjan.

Core Idea
Muawiyah warned rebels of divine consequence, but Zayd countered with pre-Islamic tribal pride.

The passage continues with an account of how Muawiyah treated the expelled rebels, trying to convince them to abandon their rebellion. The exchange with Zayd bin Sawjan reveals the deep tensions between Islamic authority and tribal identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the part about Muawiyah and the rebels. Notice how he tries to win them over with kindness and warnings.
Muawiyah's approach is diplomatic: he brings them close, feeds them, and reminds them of God.
2
Analyze Muawiyah's arguments
Muawiyah warns them against splitting the community (شق العصا) and killing the imam, emphasizing the sanctity of the imamate.
He uses religious and social arguments to discourage rebellion.
3
Examine Zayd's response
Zayd bin Sawjan retorts by criticizing Quraysh's reliance on trade while Arabs fight. This shows the tribal resentment against Qurayshite privilege.
Zayd's words reflect pre-Islamic tribal pride.
4
Reflect on Muawiyah's rebuke
Muawiyah strongly condemns Zayd's remark as a return to Jahiliyyah, showing the clash between Islamic unity and tribal identity.
This exchange illustrates the challenges of early Islamic politics.

?

?

?
The passage exposes a deep conflict: loyalty to one's group vs. loyalty to Islam. Think of a real relationship (family, friends, workplace) where you feel pressure to choose sides. Will you today consciously choose Islamic values over tribal instinct—even if it costs you status or approval? The Rebellion Against Uthman: Causes and Leaders
Baqillani recounts the causes and leaders of the rebellion against Uthman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for condemning the rebels against Uthman and the historical context of their motivations and leadership.

Core Idea
The rebels against Uthman are condemned even if he were guilty; their motives were worldly.

Baqillani presents a detailed historical account that serves both as a theological condemnation of the rebels and a factual narrative of the events leading to Uthman's murder. The passage shows that the rebels' actions were unjustifiable, and it names the key instigators, revealing that the rebellion was driven by personal grievances and ambition rather than religious principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condemnation argument
The passage begins by establishing that the rebels must be declared corrupters (fasiqun) for killing Uthman, even if he had been deserving of execution.
This is a strong statement of their culpability.
2
Examine the rebels' grievances
The author lists the reasons for the rebellion: desire for authority, anger at Uthman's governors, and personal grudges. Note that these are worldly motives, not religious.
3
Identify the leaders
The passage names the key leaders from Egypt, Basra, and Kufa, including al-Ghafiqi, Kinana ibn Bishr, Sudan ibn Hamran, Hakam ibn Jabala, and Malik al-Ashtar.
These were the figures who incited the rebellion.
4
Follow the interaction with Muawiya
Read how Muawiya initially tried to win them over with kindness and religious admonition, but Zayd ibn Suhan's rude reply led to their banishment.
This shows the rebels' stubbornness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عثمان ولا إقامة أقل الحدود على أقل الناس قدراً فكيف بعثمان مع ما ذكرناه من أحواله؟ وهذا واضح في أن القوم يجب تفسيقهم بقتله لو كان ممن يستحق القتل فضلاً عن أن يكون غير مستحق له وقد ذكرنا في غير هذا الكتاب أسباب هؤلاء القوم الذين عَدَوا عليه وكل واحد منهم والذي بعثهم على السير إليه وأنها كانت أحقاداً عليه لأجل إمْرَةٍ طلبوها ولأجل غيظ منهم على أمرائه ولأن بعضهم كان طفلاً في حَجْرِه ولأن بعضهم حرمه بعض طلبته إلى غير ذلك مما لا حاجة بنا إلى ذكره . وعبد الله وقادة هؤلاء القوم الذين أشاروا عليهم وحملوهم على ما فعلوه الغافقي المصري إمام القوم، وكنانة بن بشر التجيبي، وسودان بن حمران، بن بدیل بن ورقاء الخُزاعي، ومن قادة البصريين : حكم بن جبلة العبدي فيمن صحبه منهم ، ومن أهل الكوفة : ملك بن الحارث الأشتر النخعي في رجال قد سميناهم. وقد كان هؤلاء أثاروا الفتنة مُدة قبل قتل عثمان رضي الله عنه ورأى من الرأي إبعادهم عن المدينة فأخرج منهم عن المدينة سبعة عشر نفساً، منهم القوم الذين سميناهم ومنهم على ما ذكر صعصعة بن صوجان وزید بن صوجان العبديان وعبد الله بن الكواء، وعمرو بن الحمق، في آخرين.. فكان معاوية يقربهم ويدنيهم ويحضرهم طعامه ويُكثر إذكارهم بالله ويخوفهم شق العصا والفتك بإمام الأمة وتعظيم حرمة الإمامة ووجوب لزوم الجماعة إلى أن قال له زيد بن صوجان يوماً: «كم تكثر علينا بالإمْرَةِ ،وبقريش فوالله ما زالت العرب تأكل من قوائم سيوفها وقريش تأكل من متاجرها؛ فقال له معاوية: «اسكت لا أم لك أذكرك بالإسلام وتذكرني بالجاهلية؟ قبح الله من كثر على أمير المؤمنين بكم، فلستم برجال ضُرّ ولا نفع أخرجوا حيث شئتم»؛ فأبعدهم
Uthman (عثمان) and not establishing the least of legal punishments upon the least of people in worth—so how about Uthman, given what we have mentioned of his circumstances? And this is clear that the people must be declared corrupters (تفسيقهم) for killing him even if he were deserving of execution, let alone if he were not deserving of it. And we have mentioned in other books the reasons of these people who transgressed against him, each one of them, and what motivated them to march to him—that they had grudges against him due to a position of authority they sought, and due to anger from them against his governors, and because one of them had been a child under his guardianship, and because one of them had been denied some of his requests—and other reasons we need not mention. And Abdullah (عبد الله). The leaders of these people who advised them and incited them to what they did: al-Ghafiqi (الغافقي) al-Misri, the imam of the people; Kinana ibn Bishr al-Tujibi (كنانة بن بشر التجيبي); Sudan ibn Hamran (سودان بن حمران), [son of] Badil ibn Warqa’ al-Khuza’i (بن بدیل بن ورقاء الخُزاعي); and from the leaders of the Basrans: Hakam ibn Jabala al-Abdi (حكم بن جبلة العبدي) among those who accompanied him; and from the people of Kufa: Malik ibn al-Harith al-Ashtar al-Nakha’i (ملك بن الحارث الأشتر النخعي) among men we have named. And these people had stirred up sedition for a period before the killing of Uthman (رضي الله عنه). And he saw fit to expel them from Medina, so he expelled from among them from Medina seventeen persons, including the people we have named, and including, as mentioned, Sa’sa’a ibn Suhan (صعصعة بن صوجان) and Zayd ibn Suhan (زید بن صوجان) al-Abdiyan, and Abdullah ibn al-Kawa’ (عبد الله بن الكواء), and Amr ibn al-Humq (عمرو بن الحمق), among others. And Muawiya (معاوية) would bring them near, draw them close, invite them to his food, frequently remind them of Allah, frighten them with splitting the staff (شق العصا) and assassinating the imam of the ummah, and the veneration of the sanctity of the imamate and the obligation of adhering to the community, until Zayd ibn Suhan said to him one day: 'How often do you burden us with the matter of authority (الإمرة) and Quraysh? By Allah, the Arabs have not ceased eating from the hilts of their swords while Quraysh eat from their trade.' So Muawiya said to him: 'Be quiet, may you have no mother! I remind you of Islam and you remind me of the Jahiliyya? May Allah disfigure whoever burdens the Commander of the Faithful with you! For you are not men of harm or benefit—go out wherever you wish.' So he banished them.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a historical argument and narrative. It begins with a prior point about Uthman: if even the least of people deserves punishment according to Islamic law, then Uthman—given his virtuous status—certainly did not deserve execution. Therefore, those who killed him must be declared corrupters (fasiqun), even if he had been deserving of execution, let alone if he was not. The author then references his other works for the detailed motivations of the rebels, summarizing them as grudges related to sought-after authority, anger at Uthman's governors, and personal grievances—one was a child under Uthman's care, another was denied a request. The leader of the entire group is named as al-Ghafiqi al-Misri, followed by Kinana ibn Bishr, Sudan ibn Hamran, and the Basran leader Hakam ibn Jabala, Kufan leader Malik ibn al-Ashtar, among others. These people had been stirring sedition long before the assassination. Uthman wisely expelled seventeen of them from Medina, including those named and others like Sa'sa'a ibn Suhan, Zayd ibn Suhan, Abdullah ibn al-Kawa', and Amr ibn al-Humq. When they went to Muawiya, Muawiya initially tried to win them over with kindness and religious admonition, warning them against rebellion and violation of the imamate. However, Zayd ibn Suhan's insolent remark about Quraysh living off trade while Arabs fought prompted Muawiya to denounce him and banish them abruptly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage provides justification for the rebels by listing their grievances.
Correction: The passage lists grievances only to show they were worldly, and it explicitly condemns the rebels as fasiqun.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the rebels be declared corrupters (fasiqun) for killing Uthman?
A) Because Uthman was innocent and undeserving of execution
B) Because even if Uthman deserved execution, killing him would still be wrong
C) Because the rebels acted out of personal grudges
D) Both B and C are implied

Key Takeaway
The rebels against Uthman are unequivocally condemned, and their motives were personal and worldly, not religious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a leader make mistakes or act unjustly, what are the hidden desires in your heart that might tempt you to rebel or speak against them? How can you guard against mixing personal ambition with genuine concern for justice?
?
The rebels justified their actions by pointing to Uthman's faults, yet the passage reveals their true motives were worldly. Reflect on your own life: have you ever used someone else's shortcomings to justify your own sinful behavior or to pursue a personal agenda? What would it take for you to repent from that and return to the path of sincerity?
?
Muawiya reminded the expelled rebels of the sanctity of the imamate and the obligation of unity. How does this lesson challenge you to submit to legitimate authority—even when you disagree or feel wronged—and to prefer the unity of the community over your own desires?

The Rebels' False Repentance and Relapse
Historic narrative of rebel capture, repentance, and relapse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the events of the rebels' arrest, their insincere repentance, and their swift return to sedition, and the lesson this teaches about hypocrisy and fitna.

Core Idea
The rebels' repentance was a ruse; they returned to worse sedition, exposing their true nature.

This passage from Baqillani relates a critical episode in the conflict surrounding Uthman. After being arrested and humiliated, the rebels outwardly repented, but their hearts remained corrupt. Their betrayal upon returning to their towns reveals the depth of their hypocrisy and the persistent threat of fitna.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the sequence of events: Muawiya's report, Uthman's order, the arrest by Abd al-Rahman, the year of feigned repentance, and the final relapse.
Pay attention to the harsh words of Abd al-Rahman – they reveal his awareness of their insincerity.
2
Identify key figures and their roles
List the main characters: Uthman, Muawiya, Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Walid, Malik ibn al-Ashtar, and the rebels. Understand each one's actions and motivations as described.
Abd al-Rahman's lineage (son of Khalid ibn al-Walid) is emphasized to show his authority.
3
Trace the pattern of repentance and betrayal
Observe the cycle: the rebels show repentance and regret, are given a second chance, but then immediately return to even worse sedition. This is a key lesson about the nature of hypocrisy.
The phrase 'started something greater than what they had repented from' is crucial.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider what this story teaches about judging outward actions versus inner intentions. The rebels' oaths and tears were not genuine, as proven by their actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كتب إلى عثمان بأن القوم قد أثاروا الفتنة بالشام وقد خفت انخراق الأمر، فإن أمَرْتَ أنفذتُ إليك برؤوسهم وإلا فم فكتب إلي: «إن الفتنة قد اطلعت رأسها وألاّ يُنكاً ؛ بتسريحهم ؛ القرح ، فسحهم إلي (١)»؛ فأخرجهم معاوية؛ فعدا عليهم عبد الرحمن بن الوليد (٣)، وكان يومئذ أمير الجزيرة وكثير مما يتصل بها، فلم يفلت أحد منهم فلما أدخلوا عليه قال لهم: لا مرحبا بكم ولا أهلاً يا حزب الشيطان قد انصرف الشيطان محسوراً وأنتم في ضلالكم تترددون أنا عبد الرحمن أنا ابن خالد بن الوليد أنا ابن فاقىء عين الرِّدَّةِ لم لا تقولون لي ما كنتم تقولونه لمعاوية وعبد الله بن عثمان؟»، وقمعهم وحصرهم فكان كلما ركب أمشاهم بين يديه ثم يقول لملك بن الأشتر: «أعلِمْتَ يا ملك أن من لم يصلحه الخير أصلحه الشر؟ والله لأحسن تقويمكم» (٣)؛ فكانوا عنده سنة يظهرون التوبة والندامة من الطعن على عثمان والإثارة للفتنة وتشعيب الكلمة . وكتب إلى عثمان بتوبتهم فكتب إليـه أن سرحهم إليَّ فلما مثلوا بين يدي عثمان جددوا التوبة والندم وحلفوا له على ذلك، فخيرهم البلاد؛ فاختار بعضهم الكوفة واختار بعضهم البصرة فأخرجهم إلى حيث آثروا . فما استقرت بهم البلاد حتى شرعوا في أعظم مما تابوا منه بأنفس.
Then he wrote to Uthman (عثمان) that the people had stirred up sedition in Syria (الشام) and he feared a breach in the matter. 'If you command, I will send you their heads; otherwise...?' So he wrote to him: 'Indeed sedition has raised its head, and there is no way to heal the wound except by sending them to me.' So Muawiya (معاوية) expelled them. Then Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Walid (عبد الرحمن بن الوليد) attacked them—and he was at that time governor of the Jazira (الجزيرة) and much adjoining it—and not one of them escaped. When they were brought in before him, he said to them: 'No welcome to you, and no greeting, O party of Satan (حزب الشيطان)! Satan has returned disappointed while you linger in your error. I am Abd al-Rahman, I am the son of Khalid ibn al-Walid, I am the son of the one who put out the eye of apostasy (رِدَّة)! Why do you not say to me what you used to say to Muawiya and Abdullah ibn Uthman?' And he subdued and confined them, so that whenever he rode, he would make them walk before him. Then he would say to Malik ibn al-Ashtar: 'Know, O Malik, that he whom good does not reform, evil will reform? By Allah, I will straighten you properly!' So they remained with him for a year, outwardly showing repentance and regret for their attacks on Uthman, stirring sedition, and splitting the word. And he wrote to Uthman about their repentance, so Uthman wrote to him: 'Send them to me.' When they stood before Uthman, they renewed repentance, regret, and swore oaths to that effect. So he gave them a choice of lands: some chose Kufa, some chose Basra, and he sent them out to where they preferred. But no sooner had they settled in those lands than they began something greater than what they had repented from.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage continues the narrative. After Muawiya expelled the rebels, he wrote to Uthman informing him of the sedition in Syria and offering to execute them. Uthman replied that the only way to heal the wound was to send them to him. Muawiya then sent them out, but they were intercepted by Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Walid, governor of Jazira, who arrested all of them. Abd al-Rahman humiliated them, rebuking them harshly as the 'party of Satan' and reminding them that he was the son of Khalid ibn al-Walid, the hero who suppressed apostasy. He forced them to walk before his horse and threatened Malik ibn al-Ashtar that 'whom good does not reform, evil will reform.' The rebels stayed with him for a year, outwardly showing repentance for their attacks on Uthman and their sedition. Abd al-Rahman reported their repentance to Uthman, who ordered them brought to Medina. There they repented again, swearing oaths, so Uthman gave them a choice of settlement: some went to Kufa, some to Basra. However, as soon as they settled, they began an even greater sedition than what they had repented from.

Misconception: The rebels' repentance was sincere and they reformed.
Correction: The text states that after settling, they began something greater than what they had repented from, showing their repentance was insincere.

Key Takeaway
The rebels' outward repentance was false; they immediately returned to greater sedition, demonstrating the persistence of fitna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made a show of repentance—perhaps in public or to others—while your heart remained attached to the sin? What would it take for you to genuinely turn back, not just outwardly but in your innermost desires?
?
The rebels immediately returned to worse sedition after their supposed repentance. Where in your life have you found yourself slipping back into old habits or patterns after promising to change? What does that pattern reveal about the sincerity of your own past resolutions?
?
How can you distinguish between a repentance that is truly accepted by Allah and one that is merely a tactic or a temporary relief? What concrete signs of transformation would validate your own tawbah?

Condemnation of Rebellion Against a Legitimate Caliph
Baqillani narrates the assassination of Uthman and condemns it as rebellion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the assassination of Uthman is considered a grave injustice that undermines the unity and authority of the Muslim community.

Core Idea
Rebellion against a rightful caliph is never an act of religious reform.

This passage from Imam Baqillani's Tamhid al-Awa'il describes the tragic events of Uthman's assassination and then delivers a powerful condemnation. Baqillani argues that the attackers' actions were far from the Islamic duty of enjoining good and forbidding evil; instead, they caused disunity and weakened the religion. Understanding this condemnation is key to the Sunni position on the sanctity of the caliphate and the prohibition of armed rebellion.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, paying attention to the details of the attack, the names of the perpetrators, and the brutality described. Note that the narrative is followed by a shift to theological judgment.
The poet's lines at the beginning are a prelude to the story.
2
Identify Baqillani's main argument
Find the sentence where Baqillani states that the attack is not part of 'enjoining good and forbidding evil' but rather the opposite. This is the core of his condemnation.
Look for the phrase 'ليس من الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر في شيء'.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does Baqillani emphasize that even if Uthman had apostatized or committed adultery, the attack would still be unjustified? This shows the absolute prohibition of rebellion against a caliph.
The principle is that the means (rebellion, violence) are worse than any possible sin by the ruler.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي ذلك يقول الشاعر: لا ترْثِيَن لحزمِيّ رأيت به ضُرّاً ولو قذف الحزمي في النار الناخشين بمروان بذي خُشُب والمُقْحمين على عثمان في الدار وكان النَّفَرُ، الذين ذُكِرَ أنهم هجموا عليه من المعروفين دون أتباعهم، الغافقي وكنانة بن بشر التجيبي وسودان بن حُمران، وعبد الله بن بديل بن ورقاء، وعمرو بن الحمق الخزاعي في آخرين منهم محمد بن أبي بكر فتسرع إليه محمد وألقاه لجنبه، وجلس على صدره وأخذ لحيته فهزها وغلظ له في القول وذُكِرَ أنه ضرب جبهته بمشقَص كان في يده فلما أراد أن يُثنى وعظه عثمان وقال له : يعز على أبيك أن ترقى هذا المرقى واستحيا وانصرف وذكر أنه لم يمسه في بعض الروايات فعرف الغافقي وكنانة أنـه انصرف حياءً منه فأقحما عليه وبدره التجيبي بضربة ألقاه منها لجنبه والمصحف في حجره فلما سقط الدم عليه أطبقه ثم نحاه وضربه غيره. وقاتل البقية قوم من بني عبد الدار فقتل منهم سوى عثمان أربعة نفر وقتل العبد الأسود وارتُتْ مروان بن الحكم خارج الدار وأُنْخِنَ الحسن حتى حُمِل مغلوباً بألم الجراح. ولما رأت نائلة بنت الفرافصة، زوج عثمان وقع السيف برزت وألقت نفسها عليه فأصابتها ضربة اندرتْ من يدها ثلاث أصابع، وضرب بعض أولئك الفجرة يده عليها وقال: «ما أكبر عجيزتها، نقلونيها وصاح الآخرون ألحقوا بيت المال وأغاروا بديئاً على رَحْلِ عثمان وما كان في داره ثم تناولوا ما أمكنهم أخذه من بيت المال وأضرموا الدار عليه بالنار فاحترق أكثر أبوابها. وذُكر أن عمرو بن الحَمِقِ قال : «طعنتُ طعنات منها ثلاث الله وست لغير الله». وقد علم كلُّ مسلم أنصف نفسه أن ما فعله القـوم بـه ليس من الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر في شيء وأنه أبعد الأمور عن الحق وعن مصلحة الأمة وأدعاها إلى تفريق الكلمة وشتات الرأي وتوهين الدين والإذلال لسلطان المسلمين والتَّوَثْب على أئمتهم وتوخّي إضعاف أمرهم وقصر هممهم وأيديهم عن إقامة الدين وتنفيذ أحكام المسلمين وأن عثمان رضي الله عنه لو كان في الحقيقة قد ارتد عن دينه أو زنى بعد إحصانه لم يستحق سفك دمه على ذلك الوجه وإحراقهم داره ونهب تراثه والمطالبة بتنفيل زوجته وهذا
And concerning that the poet says: Do not weep for a determined man if you see harm upon him, even if he is thrown into the fire. Those who push Mardan with wooden sticks and those who force [entry] upon Uthman in the house. And the group who were mentioned as having stormed him, among the well-known without their followers: al-Ghafiqi, Kinana ibn Bashr al-Tujibi, Sudan ibn Human, Abdullah ibn Budayl ibn Warqa', and Amr ibn al-Hamiq al-Khuza'i, and others among them Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr. Then Muhammad rushed to him and threw him on his side, sat on his chest, took his beard, shook it, and spoke harshly to him. And it was mentioned that he struck his forehead with an arrowhead he had in his hand. When he wanted to turn away, Uthman admonished him and said: 'It is hard on your father that you should ascend this ascent.' And he became shy and left, and it was mentioned that he did not touch him in some narrations. Then al-Ghafiqi and Kinana knew that he left out of shyness from him, so they pushed against him. And al-Tujibi preceded him with a blow that threw him on his side while the Quran was in his lap. When blood fell on it, he closed it, then removed it, and others struck him. And the rest fought against a group from Banu Abd al-Dar, and four persons besides Uthman were killed, and the black slave was killed, and Marwan ibn al-Hakam was wounded outside the house, and al-Hasan was wounded until he was carried away overcome by the pain of the wounds. And when Naila bint al-Farafisa, the wife of Uthman, saw the sword fall, she came out and threw herself upon him. A blow hit her and cut off three fingers from her hand. And one of those wicked men struck her with his hand and said: 'How big her posterior is! Hand her over to us!' And others shouted: 'Go to the treasury and plunder!' And they immediately pillaged the belongings of Uthman and what was in his house. Then they took whatever they could from the treasury and set the house on fire upon him, and most of its doors burned. And it was mentioned that Amr ibn al-Hamiq said: 'I stabbed nine stabs: three for God and six for other than God.' And every Muslim who is fair to himself knows that what the people did to him is not part of enjoining good and forbidding evil in any way, and that it is the furthest matter from the truth and from the welfare of the community, and most likely to cause division of the word, dispersion of opinion, weakening of religion, humiliation of the authority of the Muslims, rebellion against their leaders, seeking to weaken their command, and hindering their ambitions and hands from establishing religion and executing the laws of the Muslims. And even if Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, had actually apostatized from his religion or committed adultery after being married, he would not have deserved his blood to be shed in that manner, nor to have his house burned, his inheritance plundered, and his wife claimed as booty. And this is...
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative followed by a theological condemnation. It describes the assassination of Uthman ibn Affan, the third caliph, by a group of rebels. The author details the key attackers and their actions: Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr, al-Ghafiqi, Kinana, Sudan, Abdullah, and Amr. The narrative emphasizes the brutality: Uthman was thrown down, his beard was shaken, his forehead struck, and eventually killed despite the Quran in his lap. The author then explicitly condemns these actions, stating that they are not 'enjoining good and forbidding evil' but rather the opposite: they cause disunity, weaken religion, and rebel against legitimate authority. The author argues that even if Uthman had committed grave sins like apostasy or adultery, the attackers' methods (burning his house, looting, seizing his wife) would not be justified. The passage thus uses history to defend the sanctity of the caliph and condemn rebellion.

Key Terms
عثمان
الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر
سفك الدم
بيت المال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attackers were justified because they were motivated by religious reform and enjoining good.
Correction: Baqillani explicitly states that their actions were not part of enjoining good and forbidding evil; instead, they caused division and weakened the religion, and even if Uthman had sinned, the violence was unjustified.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what makes the attack on Uthman unjustifiable?
A) Uthman was a righteous caliph who had not sinned.
B) The attackers did not follow proper legal procedures.
C) The attack was not an act of enjoining good and forbidding evil, but rather caused disunity and weakened Islam.
D) The attackers were not sincere in their intentions.

Key Takeaway
Rebellion against a legitimate caliph is never justified, even if the caliph commits grave sins, because such actions destroy communal unity and the authority of religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone you believe is wrong, do you ever feel a burning desire to 'set things right' that could lead you to harsh words or actions? Where do you draw the line between correcting a wrong and causing greater harm? This lesson forces you to check your heart: is your activism for Allah or for your own sense of justice?
?
The attackers claimed to be acting for God, yet they violated the sacred honor of a caliph and his family. Have you ever used religious language to justify something that deep down you knew was not pleasing to Allah? What would it take for you to stop and reconsider before acting?
?
If Uthman, a Companion and caliph, could be treated so brutally even by people claiming religious reform, how much more careful must you be when criticizing others in your life? Take a moment and think of a person you have 'written off' or are harsh towards. Would you be able to say to Allah that your treatment of them is truly for His sake and not from your ego?

The Brutal Assault on Caliph Uthman
Al-Baqillani narrates the murder of Uthman in graphic detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical details of the attack on Caliph Uthman and the immediate injustice of the act.

Core Idea
The murder of Uthman was a barbaric act, far from any legitimate Islamic practice.

Al-Baqillani provides a harrowing account of the assassination of Caliph Uthman. This lesson recounts the events as he narrates them, highlighting the violence and sacrilege involved. Understanding these details is crucial for appreciating why such acts are condemned in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Pay attention to the sequence of events and the individuals involved.
Notice the graphic details: the blood on the mushaf, the wounds, the plundering.
2
Identify key figures
List the people mentioned: al-Tajibi, al-Ghafiqi, Kinana, Na'ila, Amr ibn al-Hamiq, etc. Note their actions.
3
Reflect on the statement of Amr ibn al-Hamiq
Amr claimed some stabs were for God and others for personal reasons. Think about what this reveals about the attackers' motives.
This statement highlights the mixture of piety and personal vengeance.

أن ما فعله القوم به ليس من الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر في شيء وأنه أبعد الأمور عن الحق وعن مصلحة الأمة وأدعاها إلى تفريق الكلمة وشتات الرأي وتوهين الدين والإذلال لسلطان المسلمين والتَّوَثْب على أئمتهم وتوخّي إضعاف أمرهم وقصر هممهم وأيديهم عن إقامة الدين وتنفيذ أحكام المسلمين وأن عثمان رضي الله عنه لو كان في الحقيقة قد ارتد عن دينه أو زنى بعد إحصانه لم يستحق سفك دمه على ذلك الوجه وإحراقهم داره ونهب تراثه والمطالبة بتنفيل زوجته وهذا أظهر وأبين من أن نحتاج فيه إلى إقامة الدليل على ظلم فاعله وتعديه. وعلى أن ذلك أجمع لو وجب عليه وكان من حدود الله تعالى في شريعة لم يكن لهؤلاء القوم توليه وإقامته وإنما ذلك إلى سلطان المسلمين ولا أقل من أن يكون للأماثل منهم أهل الحل والعقد ومن يُقَدَّرُ فيه الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر دون من ذكرناه. وكل من ادعي عليه قتله من الصحابة فقد كُذِبَ عليه وأضيف إليه ما ليس منه بسبيل كعلي وطلحة والزبير رضوان الله عليهم وما من هؤلاء أجمع أحد إلا وقد أظهر المطالبة بدمه ولعن قتلته وأظهر البراءة منهم والتأسف على ألا يمكنه إقامة الحق عليهم. وهذا ظاهر معلوم من حال عليّ وطَلْحَةَ والزبير وما وراء ذلك إنما هو من الشواذ وأخبار الآحاد ولا يحل لمسلم اتقى الله وعرف قدر الصحابة أن يضيف إلى أحد منهم قتل عثمان والرضا به والخذلان مع دعائه إلى نُصرَتِه والدفع عنه بأخبارِ آحاد يعارضها مثلها تارة وما هو أقوى منها أخرى ويكون الظاهر من قول عليّ وطلحة والزبير بخلافها وفي نقيضها لأن الظاهر المعلوم لا يُتْرَكُ لروايات غير معلومة بل لو لم يظهر منهم مثل هذا لكانت مثل هذه الأخبار مدفوعة بما ورد من أمثالها في نقيضها ومعارضتها وكيف يجوز لذي علم ودين أن يضيف إلى أدونِ الصحابة بعض ما يوجب الفسق من قتل عثمان أو التأليب عليه أو خذلانه بروايات لا تساوي مدادها ولا الاشتغال بالإصغاء إليها وقد ظهر عُدْوَان من ذكرناه عليه وتوليه لقتله وفي ذلك يقول الشاعر: ألا إن خير الناس بعد ثلاثة قَتِيلُ التجيبي الذي جاء من مصر وذكر أيضاً محمد بن أبي بكر وغيره في أشعار كثيرة مشهورة، وقد ذكرناها في غير هذا الكتاب.
And he turned away and mentioned that he did not touch him in some narrations, so al-Ghafiqi and Kinana knew that he turned away out of shyness, so they rushed against him, and al-Tajibi struck him with a blow that threw him on his side with the mushaf in his lap. When blood fell on it, he closed it, then pushed it aside, and others struck him. The remaining group fought from Banu Abd al-Dar, and four of them were killed besides Uthman, and the black slave was killed, and Marwan ibn al-Hakam was wounded outside the house, and al-Hasan was injured until he was carried overwhelmed by the pain of the wounds. When Na'ila bint al-Furafisa, the wife of Uthman, saw the sword, she came out and threw herself upon him, and a blow struck her, cutting off three of her fingers. Some of those wicked ones struck his hand saying: 'How large is her posterior! Extricate her for us!' And others called out: 'Go to the treasury!' and they initially plundered Uthman's belongings and what was in his house, then they took what they could from the treasury, and set fire to his house, and most of its doors were burned. It was mentioned that Amr ibn al-Hamiq said: 'I stabbed him with stabs: three for God and six for other than God.' And every Muslim who is fair to himself knows that what these people did to him is in no way part of enjoining good and forbidding evil, and it is the farthest thing from truth and from the welfare of the umma, and the most conducive to division of the word, dispersion of opinion, weakening of religion, humiliation of the authority of the Muslims, aggression against their imams, seeking to weaken their command, and curtailing their ambitions and hands from establishing religion and executing the judgments of the Muslims. And if Uthman, may God be pleased with him, had actually apostatized from his religion or committed adultery after being married, he would not have deserved to have his blood shed in that manner, nor their burning his house, plundering his inheritance, and demanding his wife. This is more apparent and clearer than that we need to establish proof of the injustice and transgression of its doer. And even if all that had been incumbent upon him and had been among the limits of God in the Sharia, it was not for these people to undertake it and execute it; rather that is for the authority of the Muslims, at the very least for the notable ones among them, the people of binding and loosening, and those who are considered for enjoining good and forbidding evil, not for those we mentioned. And whoever among the Companions is accused of killing him has been slandered, and something that is not from him has been attributed to him, like Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr, may God be pleased with them. There is not one among all of them who did not manifest demanding vengeance for his blood, cursing his killers, manifesting disassociation from them, and regretting that he could not establish the truth against them. This is apparent and known from the condition of Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr. What is beyond that is only from the anomalous reports and isolated reports. It is not permissible for a Muslim who fears God and knows the standing of the Companions to attribute to any of them the killing of Uthman, or being pleased with it, or abandoning him despite his call for help and defense, based on isolated reports that are contradicted by similar reports sometimes, and by stronger reports other times, and the apparent statements of Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr are contrary to them and in opposition to them, because the apparent known is not abandoned for unknown narrations. Even if such statements had not appeared from them, such reports would be rejected due to the opposite reports that contradict them. How can a person of knowledge and religion attribute to the least of the Companions something that necessitates sinfulness, such as killing Uthman or inciting against him or abandoning him, based on reports that are not worth their ink nor the effort of listening to them, when the transgression of those we mentioned against him and their undertaking his killing is apparent? And about that, the poet said: 'Indeed, the best of people after three is the one killed by al-Tajibi who came from Egypt.' And Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr and others are also mentioned in many famous poems, and we have mentioned them in other than this book.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a historical and theological defense of the Companions, especially Ali, Talha, and Zubayr, against accusations of involvement in the murder of Caliph Uthman. The author begins by narrating the attack on Uthman in detail: how al-Tajibi struck him, how blood fell on the mushaf, and how Na'ila was wounded defending him. He then refutes any justification for the attackers' actions, arguing that even if Uthman had committed a sin such as apostasy or adultery, the punishment would not have been mob lynching, and the attackers were not authorized to carry out divine limits. Such authority belongs to the Muslim ruler and the people of binding and loosening (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd). He then addresses accusations against the Companions: anyone claiming that Ali, Talha, or Zubayr participated in the killing is lying, because their known stance was to demand justice, curse the killers, and dissociate from them. The author dismisses any contrary reports as anomalous (shawadh) and isolated (akhbar ahad), which are outweighed by the well-known facts and stronger reports. He concludes that it is impermissible for a Muslim who fears God and respects the Companions to attribute such evil to them based on weak reports, especially when the transgression of the actual perpetrators is evident. The passage ends with a poetic verse and a reference to more details in another book.

Key Terms
الصحابة
المصحف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani's account, what was Uthman holding when he was struck by al-Tajibi?
A) A sword
B) The Quran (mushaf)
C) A shield
D) A letter

Key Takeaway
The assassination of Uthman was a heinous act involving violence, sacrilege, and plunder.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture Uthman, the Prophet's companion, holding the Quran while being struck down, do you feel a sense of outrage? What does that outrage reveal about your own attachment to the Quran and the companions? Will you allow this story to deepen your reverence for the mushaf or let it remain just a historical fact?
?
Amr ibn al-Hamiq claimed to have done some stabs 'for God' and some 'for other than God.' In what hidden areas of your life do you mix your own desires with acts you claim are for Allah? Take a moment to identify one such area and commit to purifying that intention today.
?
The attackers justified their actions with a cause, yet they ended up plundering, burning houses, and mutilating. If you were in a heated dispute with fellow Muslims, what would stop you from crossing into similar evil? Examine your heart now—where are you most vulnerable to justifying sin in the name of righteousness?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
Mob Justice is Never Justified in Islam
Al-Baqillani argues that the attack on Uthman was not enjoining good and that punishment is only for the Muslim authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why even if a Muslim ruler commits a sin, it is not permissible for individuals to take the law into their own hands.

Core Idea
Punishing a ruler is the duty of the Muslim authority, not the mob.

After recounting the murder, al-Baqillani argues that the actions of the attackers cannot be justified under any Islamic principle. Even if Uthman had committed a major sin, the penalty would not be execution by a mob. This lesson examines the legal and moral arguments against vigilante justice in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. The author makes a hypothetical argument about Uthman's hypothetical sins.
Notice the conditional structure: 'لو كان ... لم يستحق ...'
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
The author: 1) rejects that the act was enjoining good, 2) states even if sin, such punishment is not deserved, 3) says authority lies with the ruler and ahl al-hall wa al-aqd.
3
Reflect on the principle
Think about why this principle is important for political stability and justice in Islam.
The author emphasizes that mob action leads to division, weakening of religion, and destruction of authority.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any Muslim can punish a sinful ruler.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, who is authorized to carry out divine punishments (hudud) against a Muslim ruler?
A) Any individual Muslim who witnesses wrongdoing
B) The family of the accused
C) The Muslim authority (sultan al-muslimeen) and the people of binding and loosening
D) The tribal leaders

Key Takeaway
No individual or mob has the right to execute Islamic punishments; that authority lies with the Muslim ruler and the qualified scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness injustice among your community or leaders, does your first instinct seek to take matters into your own hands or to consult with people of knowledge and authority? How does this passage correct that instinct?
?
If you were in a situation where a leader seemed guilty of a major sin, would you trust your own judgment to punish them, or would you submit to the process of legitimate authority? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to divine order versus personal righteousness?
?
Think of a time you felt outraged by someone's wrongdoing. Did your response reflect the care for the umma's unity and the rule of law that Al-Baqillani describes, or did it prioritize your emotion over the communal good? What will you change?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
Vindicating the Companions: Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr
Al-Baqillani refutes accusations against the Companions and establishes the precedence of known facts over isolated reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqillani defends the Companions from accusations of involvement in Uthman's murder and why isolated reports cannot override established facts.

Core Idea
The known stance of the Companions cannot be overturned by weak, isolated reports.

Several Companions, especially Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr, were accused of complicity in Uthman's murder. Al-Baqillani vigorously defends them, arguing that their well-known actions and statements contradict such accusations. He also establishes a important principle: isolated reports (akhbar ahad) cannot outweigh established knowledge (al-zahir al-ma'lum). This lesson explores that defense and its implications for historical reliability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The author names Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr specifically.
Notice the emphatic language: 'كُذِبَ عليه' (he has been lied about).
2
Understand the argument
The author gives the known stance of these Companions and then argues that contrary reports are anomalous and isolated.
The key phrase: 'الظاهر المعلوم لا يُتْرَكُ لروايات غير معلومة' (the apparent known is not abandoned for unknown narrations).
3
Apply the principle
Think about how this principle protects the reputation of the Companions and guides historical methodology in Islam.
The author concludes that attributing sin to any Companion based on such reports is impermissible.
