Uthman's Discipline of Ammar: False Accusation or Justified Act?
Baqillani refutes the accusation that Uthman struck Ammar, with explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's defense of Caliph Uthman against the charge of unjustly striking Ammar, and the reasoning behind considering it as justified discipline.

Core Idea
Even if the strike occurred, it was rightful discipline, not tyranny.

This passage addresses a serious accusation against Caliph Uthman: that he violently struck the Companion Ammar ibn Yasir. Baqillani refutes the charge on multiple grounds, challenging its reliability and, even if accepted, interpreting it as necessary discipline for Ammar's excessive behavior. The lesson unpacks this defense and its implications for understanding early Muslim conflicts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the accusation
Read the opening lines where the accusation is stated: Uthman struck Ammar until his intestines were torn, and also struck Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.
Note the severity of the claim.
2
Examine Baqillani's first response
Notice how Baqillani says the reports are false according to many, not compelling, and not proven.
He questions the reliability of the narrations.
3
Explore the second response
Even if true, Uthman's action must be interpreted as correct. Then read the narrated cause: Ammar spoke harshly and belittled the Imam.
4
Analyze the justification
Understand that the Imam has authority to discipline, and even if it leads to death, he is not sinful. The text concludes that either the strike is false or it was justified discipline.
This is the core of Baqillani's argument.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this defense addresses sectarian criticisms and upholds the status of Uthman as a just caliph.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذكر ما تعلقوا به على عثمان ونقموا من فعله والجواب عنه. فأما تعلقهم بأنه ضرب عماراً حتى فتق أمعاءه وضرب عبد الله أضلاعه ومنعه العطاء سنين كثيرة وأنه رد عليه مسعود حتى كسر ضلعين من عطاءه فباطل عند كثير من الناس وليس ما يروون في هذا مما نُضْطَرُّ إليه أو مما قام دليل عليه ولا مما يُثبت تعلق القوم به. وعلى أنه لو ثبت ذلك، لوجب أن يحمل فعله على الصحة. وقد رُوي أن سبب ضربه لعمار أنه قال للطاعنين عليه اكتبوا ما تشكونه من عثمان في كتاب واعطونيه حتى أدخل عليه وأوقفه عليه، فكتب ذلك ودخل عليه فغلّظ له في القول وافترى واستخف بسلطان الله وليس له ذلك، ولا يحل له ولا لغيره أن يخاطب عثمان بذلك؛ وليس بأمير المؤمنين إلا بما يقتضيه ما ذكرناه من محله في الدين ومنزلته بين المسلمين وحرمته وسابقته في الدين فكيف وهو السلطان المأمور بطاعته وترك الافتيات عليه؟ وليس فيما أنكروه عليه مُنكَر يستحق به مثل ما خرج إليه عمار . وقد رُوي أنه كان يقول: عثمان كافر؛ وكان يقول بعد قتله : قتلنا عثمان يوم قتلناه كافراً حتى قال له علي عليه السلام مرة وهو يخاصم في ذلك الحسن بن عليّ ، والحسن ينكر ذلك من قوله : «أَتَكْفُرُ يا عمار بربّ آمَنَ به عثمان؟ فقال: لا فأرسل الحَسَنُ يده من يده. وهذا ؛ سرف عظيم من خرج إلى ما هو دونه استحق الأدب من الإمام؛ فلعل عثمان انتهره وأدبه لكثرة قوله : قد خَلَعْتُ عثمان ، وأنا بريء منه» فأدى الأدب إلى فتق أمعائه. ولو أدى أدب الإمام إلى تلف النفس لم يكن بذلك مأثوماً ولا مستحقاً للخلع فإما أن يكون ضربه باطلاً وإما أن يكون صحيحاً فيكون ردعاً وتأديباً ونهياً عن الإغراق والسَّرَف وذلك صواب من فعل عثمان وهفوة من عمار
Chapter: Mention of what they attached to Uthman and criticized from his action, and the response to it. As for their attachment that he struck Ammar until he tore his intestines, and struck Abdullah's ribs and withheld his stipend for many years, and that he returned Ibn Mas'ud [؟] until he broke two ribs from his stipend, it is false according to many people. What they narrate in this is not something we are compelled to accept, nor is there proof established for it, nor does it prove their attachment. And if that were proven, then his action would necessarily be interpreted as correct. It has been narrated that the cause of his striking Ammar was that he said to those criticizing him: 'Write what you complain of from Uthman in a book and give it to me so that I may enter upon him and inform him of it.' So they wrote that and he entered upon him and spoke harshly to him, slandered, and belittled the authority of God. He has no right to do that, nor is it permissible for him or anyone else to address Uthman in that manner, and he is not the Commander of the Faithful except due to what we mentioned of his standing in religion, his rank among Muslims, his sanctity and his precedence in religion. How [can this be] when he is the ruler whose obedience is commanded and who is not to be defied? And there is nothing in what they denied of him that is objectionable deserving what Ammar went out to do. It has been narrated that he used to say: 'Uthman is an unbeliever,' and after his killing he used to say: 'We killed Uthman the day we killed him as an unbeliever,' until Ali, peace be upon him, once said to him while he was arguing with Hasan ibn Ali about that, and Hasan denied that from his statement: 'Do you disbelieve, O Ammar, in a Lord in whom Uthman believed?' He said: 'No.' So Hasan released his hand from his hand. This is great excess from one who went out against what is beneath him, deserving discipline from the Imam. Perhaps Uthman rebuked him and disciplined him due to his frequent saying: 'I have renounced Uthman, and I am innocent of him.' So the discipline led to the tearing of his intestines. If the Imam's discipline leads to loss of life, he is not thereby sinful nor deserving of removal. So either his striking is false or it is correct, being deterrence and discipline and prohibition from extremism and excess, and that is correct from Uthman's action and a lapse from Ammar.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an accusation against Caliph Uthman. The author begins by stating the chapter heading: a chapter mentioning the objections raised against Uthman and the responses. Then he addresses the specific accusation that Uthman struck Ammar ibn Yasir so severely that his intestines were ruptured, and also struck Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and withheld his stipend. The author declares this accusation false according to many people, and that the reports are not compelling nor proven. Even if true, Uthman's action must be interpreted as correct. He then narrates the reason for striking Ammar: Ammar told critics to write down their grievances so he could present them to Uthman, but when he entered, he spoke harshly and belittled Uthman. The author emphasizes that no one has the right to address the Caliph in such a manner, given his status. Ammar went further, repeatedly calling Uthman a disbeliever and even after his death saying they killed him as such. The author recounts an incident where Hasan ibn Ali challenged Ammar on this. The author concludes that Uthman's discipline was justified, and even if it led to death, the Imam is not sinful. Thus, the striking was either false or correct as discipline.

Key Terms
عثمان
عمار
فتق أمعاءه
هفوة
أدب الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Uthman's striking of Ammar was purely unjust and unprovoked.
Correction: Baqillani argues that even if the striking occurred, it was justified discipline for Ammar's disrespect and slander against the caliph, and thus not a wrongful act.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what was the reason for Uthman striking Ammar?
A) Ammar refused to pay zakat
B) Ammar spoke harshly to Uthman and belittled his authority
C) Ammar was a known heretic
D) Uthman acted without cause

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends Uthman by questioning the authenticity of the reports and justifying the act as legitimate discipline for insubordination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone in authority corrects you, do you receive it as discipline from Allah, or do you immediately accuse them of injustice? What is in your heart that makes you resist?
?
Have you ever declared a fellow Muslim to be outside the fold of Islam because of a political or personal disagreement? How does Ammar's 'lapse' challenge you to examine your own words?
?
If you were in Uthman's position and had to discipline a persistent slanderer, would you have the courage to act firmly without fearing accusations? What would you need to change in yourself to uphold justice even when criticized?

Defending Uthman's Actions Regarding Ibn Mas'ud
Baqillani defends Uthman's treatment of Ibn Mas'ud and the unification of the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's defense of Uthman's disciplinary actions against Ibn Mas'ud and the necessity of standardizing the Quranic text to prevent discord.

Core Idea
Uthman's discipline of Ibn Mas'ud was to protect the Quran, not from personal animosity.

Baqillani addresses a sensitive historical accusation: that Caliph Uthman struck Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and withheld his stipend. The author refutes this as false, but even if true, he argues it must be understood within the context of preserving the Quranic text. This passage reveals the principles of scholarly interpretation of early Muslim conflicts, focusing on intentions and the greater good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice Baqillani's rhetorical structure: initial refutation then conditional defense.
2
Identify the main accusation
The accusation is that Uthman struck Ibn Mas'ud and withheld his stipend. Baqillani declares this false.
This sets up the conditional argument that follows.
3
Analyze the conditional argument
Even if true, Baqillani interprets Uthman's action correctly: to discipline Ibn Mas'ud for refusing to hand over his mushaf.
The justification is based on Uthman's justice and the need to prevent turmoil.
4
Understand the greater good
Uthman aimed to unify the Quran and prevent differences in recitation. Ibn Mas'ud had no right to prevent that.
The passage also alludes to anomalies in Ibn Mas'ud's mushaf.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this defense uses principles of interpretation: assuming good faith in the Prophet's companions and prioritizing communal unity.
This illustrates the Sunni approach to early historical controversies.

وأما ضربه عبد الله بن مسعود ومنعه العطاء وكراهة عبد الله له فإنه باطل أيضاً غير صحيح؛ فإن صح ذلك، حمل من عثمان مع ثبوت عدالته وإيمانه على وجه صحيح وهو أن يكون قصد بذلك تأديب عبد الله ورَدْعَه عن الامتناع من إخراج المصحف إلى مثل عثمان وعلى سائر الصحابة علمه بشدة الهَرْج والفساد واختلاف القراءة وتوخي الكلمة والموافقة على مصحف متفق عليه محفوظ ومحروس وجمع يكون العِمَادَ في هذا الباب ولقد وُفِّقَ في ذلك لأمر من الدين عظيم وخير كثير فلم يكن لعبد الله أن يمنع من ذلك هذا مع العجائب التي يذكرون أنها في من إلغاء المُعوِّذَتَين وإثبات ما نُسِخَتْ تلاوته ويبعد أن يكون من مصحفه
As for his striking Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, withholding his stipend, and Abdullah's dislike of him, that is also false and not correct. If that is proven, it is interpreted from Uthman, given his justice and faith, in a correct manner: that he intended by that to discipline Abdullah and deter him from refusing to hand over the mushaf to the likes of Uthman and all the Companions, due to his knowledge of the severity of turmoil and corruption and differences in recitation, and seeking consensus and agreement on a preserved and guarded mushaf, and a compilation that would be the pillar in this matter. He was indeed guided in that to a great matter of religion and abundant good. So Abdullah had no right to prevent that, especially given the wonders they mention that are in his mushaf, such as abrogating the two Mu'awwidhat and affirming what was abrogated in recitation, and it is unlikely that it was from his mushaf.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage continues the defense of Uthman, now addressing the accusation that he struck Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and withheld his stipend. The author declares this accusation false. Even if true, Uthman's action must be interpreted correctly, given his justice and faith. The purpose was to discipline Ibn Mas'ud for refusing to hand over his personal mushaf (Quran codex) to the central committee tasked with standardizing the Quranic text. Uthman saw great turmoil and differences in recitation, and sought to create a unified, preserved mushaf. This was a great religious good. Ibn Mas'ud had no right to prevent this. Additionally, the author refers to claims that Ibn Mas'ud's mushaf omitted the two Mu'awwidhat (Surat al-Falaq and an-Nas) and included verses whose recitation was abrogated, suggesting that his mushaf was not fully reliable. Therefore, Uthman's action was justified.

Key Terms

المصحف
إلغاء المعوذتين
القراءة
الهَرْج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman struck Ibn Mas'ud out of personal animosity or political spite.
Correction: Baqillani argues that even if true, the action was disciplinary to ensure preservation of the Quran, reflecting Uthman's piety and concern for the community's unity.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why did Uthman discipline Ibn Mas'ud regarding the mushaf?
A) Ibn Mas'ud refused to pay his stipend
B) Ibn Mas'ud refused to hand over his mushaf for the unification of the Quranic text
C) Ibn Mas'ud was a known hypocrite
D) Uthman wanted to destroy all other mushafs out of spite

Key Takeaway
Uthman's actions against Ibn Mas'ud, if true, were justified by the need to standardize the Quran and prevent discord in the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever resisted a decision made for the collective good because it challenged your personal preference or understanding? How does Uthman's prioritizing of the unified mushaf over Ibn Mas'ud's personal copy confront your own attachment to 'being right'?
?
The passage says Uthman was 'guided to a great matter of religion.' Do you genuinely trust that leaders—even when their actions seem harsh—can be guided by Allah? Where in your life are you withholding trust from those in authority, and what would submission require of you?
?
Ibn Mas'ud was a beloved companion, yet he refused to hand over his mushaf. Are there cherished practices or beliefs you hold that might actually be causing division? What would it cost you to let them go for the sake of unity?

Defense of Uthman's Punishment of Ammar ibn Yasir
Baqillani defends Uthman's beating of Ammar as legitimate discipline.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that an imam's disciplinary action, even if it leads to death, can be valid and not sinful if the intention is legitimate discipline and the recipient is blameworthy.

Core Idea
An imam's discipline, even fatal, can be valid if it deters wrongdoing and the recipient is blameworthy.

This passage addresses a controversial incident in early Islamic history: Caliph Uthman's physical punishment of the Companion Ammar ibn Yasir, which reportedly caused fatal injury. Baqillani, writing from a Sunni perspective, defends Uthman by establishing the principles of legitimate imamic authority and discipline. He argues that the severity of the punishment alone does not make it sinful, and he presents a logical dichotomy to affirm Uthman's correctness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the incident
Read the passage to grasp the historical scenario: Ammar repeatedly said 'I have removed Uthman, and I am free from him,' leading to Uthman's rebuke and discipline.
Note the role of Hasan ibn Ali as an interlocutor.
2
Identify Baqillani's defense strategy
Notice how Baqillani first frames Ammar's actions as deserving discipline, then argues that even if the punishment resulted in death, the imam is not automatically sinful or deserving of removal.
The key phrase: 'ولو أدى أدب الإمام إلى تلف النفس لم يكن بذلك مأثوماً ولا مستحقاً للخلع'.
3
Analyze the dichotomy
Baqillani presents a dichotomy: either the beating was invalid (bāṭil) or correct (ṣaḥīḥ). He implicitly assumes Uthman's justice and concludes it was correct, thus a valid act of deterrence.
Consider how this rhetorical move shifts the burden of proof.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument fits into Sunni theology regarding the uprightness of the Companions and the authority of the imam. Baqillani interprets Uthman's action in the best possible light.
This reflects the Sunni principle of حسن الظن بالصحابة.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخاصم في ذلك الحَسَنَ بن عليّ ، والحسن ينكر ذلك من قوله : «أَتَكْفُرُ يا عمار بربّ آمَنَ به عثمان؟ فقال: لا فأرسل الحَسَنُ يده من يده. وهذا سرف عظيم من خرج إلى ما هو دونه استحق الأدب من الإمام؛ فلعل عثمان انتهره وأدبه لكثرة قوله: قد خَلَعْتُ عثمان، وأنا بريء منه» فأدى الأدب إلى فتق أمعائه. ولو أدى أدب الإمام إلى تلف النفس لم يكن بذلك مأثوماً ولا مستحقاً للخلع فإما أن يكون ضربه باطلاً وإما أن يكون صحيحاً فيكون ردعاً وتأديباً ونهياً عن الإغراق والسَّرَف وذلك صواب من فعل عثمان وهفوة من عمار.
He argues with Hasan ibn Ali about that, and Hasan denies that from his statement: 'Do you disbelieve, O Ammar, in a Lord in whom Uthman believed?' So he said: No. Then Hasan let go of his hand. This is extreme arrogance from someone who went beyond his station; he deserved discipline from the Imam. Perhaps Uthman rebuked and disciplined him for his frequent saying: 'I have removed Uthman, and I am free from him.' So the discipline led to the rupture of his intestines. And if the discipline of the Imam led to the destruction of a life, he is not thereby sinful nor deserving of removal from office. So either his beating was invalid or it was correct, in which case it was deterrence, discipline, and forbidding of excess and extravagance. That was correct from Uthman's action and a lapse from Ammar.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Caliph Uthman's punishment of Ammar ibn Yasir, responding to criticisms. The author (Baqillani) recounts an incident where Hasan ibn Ali argued with Ammar, who had claimed to have broken allegiance with Uthman. Ammar said 'I have broken with Uthman and I am free of him.' Uthman rebuked and disciplined him for this repeated statement, and the discipline was so severe that it caused fatal injury (rupture of intestines). The author argues that even if the imam's discipline leads to death, the imam is not sinful nor does he deserve to be removed from office, provided his intention was valid. The author presents a dichotomy: either the beating was invalid (bāṭil) or correct (ṣaḥīḥ). Since Uthman is known to be just, it must be correct; thus it was a legitimate act of deterrence (radʿ) and discipline (taʾdīb), forbidding excess (ighrāq) and extravagance (saraff). Therefore, it was a correct action from Uthman and a lapse (hafwa) from Ammar. The author emphasizes that the imam has authority to discipline, and the severity does not automatically make him blameworthy. This argument is typical of the Ahl al-Sunna defense of the Companions' actions, interpreting them in the best possible light given their established justice.

Key Terms
أدب
إمام
خلع
ردع
سرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that any injury caused by a Companion is automatically sinful or that a fatal punishment is always unjust.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the imam's discipline, even if fatal, can be justified if it serves legitimate deterrence and the recipient is blameworthy; the imam is not sinful simply because of the outcome.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's argument, what makes Uthman's beating of Ammar valid rather than invalid?
A) Ammar's statement 'I have broken with Uthman' was true, so discipline was necessary.
B) Uthman's intention was deterrence and discipline, and he acted within his authority as imam.
C) The beating did not actually cause any injury, so it was permissible.
D) Ammar was a disbeliever, so beating him was lawful.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends Uthman by establishing that the imam's legitimate disciplinary authority can justify even fatal punishment, and that Uthman's action was a correct deterrence while Ammar's claim was a lapse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself quick to justify harsh actions from leaders you revere, while condemning similar actions from those you oppose? This passage argues that an imam's discipline, even fatal, can be correct if the recipient is blameworthy. Examine your heart: are you applying this standard consistently, or does bias shape your judgment? Commit to evaluating all leaders' actions by the same criteria of justice and legitimacy.
?
When you have been corrected by authority, do you accept it as discipline or do you respond with defiance and claims of injustice? Ammar's response is described as a 'lapse' and 'extreme arrogance'. Reflect on a time you were disciplined; was your reaction one of humility or pride? Ask Allah for the strength to accept correction and to guard against arrogance.
?
The passage presents a dichotomy: punishment is either invalid or correct. Are you willing to submit to a leader's discipline even if it seems harsh, trusting that it may be for your own good? Or do you immediately assume injustice? Confront the place of obedience and trust in your relationship with legitimate authority, and repent if you have harbored rebellion based on emotion rather than principle.

Sects and Disagreements

Defense of Uthman's Treatment of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud
Baqillani interprets Uthman's beating and withholding of stipend from Ibn Mas'ud as legitimate ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani defends Uthman's actions against Ibn Mas'ud as legitimate ijtihad based on the greater good and the principle of interpreting the actions of the Companions favorably.

Core Idea
Even seemingly harsh actions by a just imam can be valid if they serve a greater religious purpose.

This passage is a detailed defense of Caliph Uthman's controversial treatment of the Companion Abdullah ibn Mas'ud. Baqillani argues that both the beating and the withholding of the stipend must be interpreted in light of Uthman's proven justice and the critical need to standardize the Quranic text. By examining the reasoning, we learn the Ashʿarī methodology of assuming the best of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and the literal English translation. Let the author's own argument unfold naturally.
Notice how Baqillani begins by dismissing the criticisms as invalid before providing possible interpretations.
2
Identify the two main incidents
The passage addresses two separate actions: Uthman's beating of Ibn Mas'ud and his denial of Ibn Mas'ud's stipend. Also note the mention of Ibn Mas'ud's dislike of Uthman.
Both are defended using the same methodology of assuming the best of the imam's ijtihad.
3
Understand the justification for the beating
Focus on the reasoning: Uthman's goal was to discipline Ibn Mas'ud for refusing to hand over his mushaf during a time of Quranic disputes, with the aim of unifying the text. This is presented as a great religious good.
Baqillani permits even severe discipline if the imam's ijtihad leads to it and he does not intend harm.
4
Recognize the principle of interpreting actions favorably
The passage concludes that such incidents cannot be used to impugn the integrity of the Imams, as they are based on ijtihad and isolated reports are insufficient for condemnation.
This reflects the Ashʿarī principle of husn al-zann (good opinion) toward the Companions.

وأما ضربه عبد الله بن مسعود ومنعه العطاء وكراهة عبد الله له فإنه باطل أيضاً غير صحيح؛ فإن صح ذلك، حمل من عثمان مع ثبوت عدالته وإيمانه على وجه صحيح وهو أن يكون قصد بذلك تأديب عبد الله ورَدْعَه عن الامتناع من إخراج المصحف إلى مثل عثمان وعلى سائر الصحابة علمه بشدة الهَرْج والفساد واختلاف القراءة وتوخي الكلمة والموافقة على مصحف متفق عليه محفوظ محروس وجمع يكون العِمَادَ في هذا الباب ولقد وُفِّقَ في ذلك لأمر من الدين عظيم وخير كثير فلم يكن لعبد الله أن يمنع من ذلك هذا مع العجائب التي يذكرون أنها في من إلغاء المُعوِّذَتَين وإثبات ما نُسِخَتْ تلاوته ويبعد أن يكون من مصحفه. وقد كان يجب أن يُخرج ما في يده ويوافقه عليه فإذا امتنع من ذلك جاز للإمام إرهابه بشيء من الضرب إذا أداه الاجتهاد إلى ذلك فإن أدى الضرب إلى كسر ضلع وإبطال عضو وإذهاب البصر لم يكن الإمام بذلك مأثوماً ولا حرجاً إذا لم يقصد إلا التأديب والتشديد وكذلك إن قرفه بظلم ومعصية أو ألب عليه ولم يضمر بذلك الحجة وكانت ظاهر الإمام العدالة جاز له تأديبه. وأما قولهم إنه كره أخذ العطاء، فلعله رأى في وقت ردَّه إلى من هو أحق منه أو لعله استغنى عنه أو لعله اعتقد أن فيه شُبُهَةٌ تمنع من أخذه وإن كان غالطاً في اعتقاده ذلك لأنه ليس بمعصوم لا يجوز الزلل عليه أو رأى أنه يستحق أكثر مما أعطاه عثمان ولم يكن يستحق عنده أكثر مما قسمه له وهذا مردود إلى اجتهاد الإمام ورأيه وليس لأحد الافتئات عليه ولا ردُّ العطاء إذا لم يرضَه أو يبين ابن مسعود أن رد العطاء لوجه يوجب فسق عثمان فيُنظر في ذلك فإن لم يكن معنى غير رده حُمِل أمره على بعض ما قلناه وكذلك ضرب عثمان إياه حَمْلُ أمره على الأليق به أولى وأما قولهم إنه منعه العطاء لسنين فإنه غير ثابت، فإن صح، فلعله كَرِه أن يأخذه أو لعل عثمان صرفه إلى غيره لأنه كان أولى منه وهو مصيب في ذلك إذا أداه اجتهاده إليه. ومثل هذا لا يثبت بأخبار الآحاد ولا يُتوصل به إلى القدح في الأئمة وفضلاء الأمة.
As for his beating of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and his denial of his stipend and Abdullah's dislike of him, it is also invalid and incorrect. If that is established, it is interpreted from Uthman, given his proven justice and faith, in a correct manner: that he intended by that to discipline Abdullah and deter him from refusing to hand over the mushaf to the likes of Uthman and to the rest of the Companions, knowing the severity of turmoil and corruption and the differences in recitation, and to seek unanimity on a preserved and protected mushaf that is agreed upon, and to collect that which is the mainstay in this matter. He was successful in that for a great matter of religion and much good. So Abdullah should not have prevented that. This is in addition to the wonders they mention that are in his causing the omission of the Mu'awwidhatayn and the inclusion of what had been abrogated in recitation. It is far-fetched that it would be from his mushaf. And it was incumbent upon him to hand over what was in his hand and agree with him. If he refused that, it is permissible for the Imam to threaten him with some beating if his ijtihad leads to that. If the beating leads to a broken rib, loss of a limb, or loss of sight, the Imam is not thereby sinful nor in error, if he intended only discipline and severity. Likewise, if he accused him of injustice and disobedience, or incited against him without concealing the argument, and the outward state of the Imam is justice, it is permissible for him to discipline him. As for their saying that he disliked taking the stipend, perhaps he saw at that time it should be returned to someone more deserving, or perhaps he was independent of it, or perhaps he believed there was some ambiguity that prevented him from taking it, even if he was mistaken in that belief because he is not infallible and error can occur upon him. Or he thought he deserved more than what Uthman gave him, but he did not deserve more than what was apportioned to him. This is referred to the ijtihad of the Imam and his opinion, and no one has the right to contest him. He may not return the stipend unless he is displeased with it, or Ibn Mas'ud makes clear that the return of the stipend was for a reason that necessitates Uthman's sin, so that would be examined. If there is no meaning other than his refusal, his matter is interpreted according to some of what we have said. Likewise, the beating of Uthman to him is interpreted in the most appropriate way. As for their saying that he denied him the stipend for years, that is not established. If it is established, perhaps he disliked taking it, or perhaps Uthman diverted it to someone else because he was more deserving, and he was correct in that if his ijtihad led to it. And such matters are not established by ahad reports, nor can they be used to disparage the Imams and the virtuous of the community.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a detailed defense of Caliph Uthman's actions toward Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, specifically regarding the beating and the withholding of his stipend. The author (Baqillani) begins by stating that criticisms of Uthman's treatment of Ibn Mas'ud are invalid. If the incident is true, it must be interpreted in a sound manner consistent with Uthman's established justice and faith. The author proposes that Uthman's intention was to discipline Ibn Mas'ud for refusing to hand over his personal copy of the Quran (mushaf). At that time, there was turmoil and differences in recitation, and Uthman sought to unify the Muslims on a standard, protected mushaf. This was a great religious good, and Ibn Mas'ud should not have refused. The author adds that some claim Ibn Mas'ud's mushaf omitted the Mu'awwidhatayn and included verses whose recitation was abrogated, making it unlikely to be authoritative. Therefore, Ibn Mas'ud was obliged to comply. If he refused, it is permissible for the imam to use threat of beating according to his ijtihad. Even if the beating causes severe injury (broken rib, loss of limb or sight), the imam is not sinful if he intended only discipline and severity. However, the imam must be outwardly just and not conceal his reasoning. Next, the author addresses the issue of the stipend. Ibn Mas'ud reportedly disliked taking the stipend. The author offers several possible interpretations: perhaps Ibn Mas'ud thought another was more deserving, or he was self-sufficient, or he doubted its permissibility due to some ambiguity, even if mistaken, since he is not infallible. Alternatively, he might have thought he deserved more than what Uthman gave him. Ultimately, such matters are the result of ijtihad by the imam, and no one has the right to oppose him. If Ibn Mas'ud's refusal of the stipend was not clearly due to Uthman's wrongdoing, then his actions are interpreted favorably. The claim that Uthman withheld the stipend for years is unsubstantiated; even if true, it may be due to Ibn Mas'ud's own refusal or because Uthman redirected it to someone more deserving. Finally, the author asserts that such reports are isolated (āḥād) and cannot be used to impugn the integrity of the Imams and the virtuous of the community. This passage exemplifies the Ashʿarī principle of interpreting the actions of the Companions in the best possible light, assuming their ijtihad is valid, and refraining from condemning them based on weak or ambiguous evidence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Uthman's harsh treatment of Ibn Mas'ud disproves his justice.
Correction: This lesson shows that such actions must be interpreted as legitimate discipline given Uthman's proven justice and the need for a standardized Quran.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what justifies Uthman's use of force against Abdullah ibn Mas'ud?
A) Ibn Mas'ud had committed apostasy.
B) Uthman was personally angry and took revenge.
C) Ibn Mas'ud refused to hand over his mushaf during a time of dispute over the Quran, and Uthman's ijtihad led him to use discipline.
D) Ibn Mas'ud had insulted Uthman publicly.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the actions of a just imam must be interpreted in the best possible light, and that isolated reports cannot override the established integrity of the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a leader making a harsh decision, do you immediately assume injustice, or do you search for a valid religious reason? What does your default assumption reveal about your trust in those in authority?
?
Have you ever stubbornly held onto a personal opinion (like Ibn Mas'ud holding his mushaf) for what you thought was a good reason, only to later realize it was better to submit for the sake of unity? How can you train yourself to prioritize the greater good of the community over your own views?
?
The lesson teaches that isolated reports cannot override the established integrity of the Companions. Are you quick to believe or spread negative stories about scholars or leaders based on weak evidence? Will you commit today to only accept well-established accounts when it comes to honoring the righteous?

The Imam's Authority to Discipline
Baqillani states the general principle of imam's disciplinary authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of an imam's authority to use disciplinary force based on ijtihad, and why even severe outcomes can be justified if the intent is correction.

Core Idea
An imam's discipline by ijtihad is not sinful even if it causes severe injury.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a key objection against Caliph Uthman: his use of beatings. He establishes a general principle that if an imam, after sincere ijtihad, deems discipline necessary, he is not sinful even if severe injury results, as long as his intent is correction. This principle sets the foundation for defending Uthman's specific actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice how Baqillani begins with a conditional statement.
2
Identify the conditions for discipline
The passage lists conditions under which an imam may use force: if the person refuses to comply, and if ijtihad leads to it. Note the permissible actions: beatings, accusations, incitement.
3
Examine the ruling on severe outcomes
Baqillani explicitly states that even if the beating breaks a rib, disables a limb, or causes blindness, the imam is not sinful or liable if his intent was discipline and severity.
This is a strong statement protecting the imam from liability.
4
Consider the intent requirement
The key factor is the imam's intention: he must not intend proof or harm, only discipline. His apparent justice also matters.
5
Reflect on application to Uthman
Think how this principle can be applied to Uthman's treatment of Ibn Mas'ud. It provides a general framework for defending Uthman's actions.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد كان يجب أن يُخرج ما في يده ويوافقه عليه فإذا امتنع من ذلك جاز للإمام إرهابه بشيء من الضرب إذا أداه الاجتهاد إلى ذلك فإن أدى الضرب إلى كسر ضلع وإبطال عضو وإذهاب البصر لم يكن الإمام بذلك مأثوماً ولا حرجاً إذا لم يقصد إلا التأديب والتشديد وكذلك إن قرفه بظلم ومعصية أو ألب عليه ولم يضمر بذلك الحجة وكانت ظاهر الإمام العدالة جاز له تأديبيه. وأما قولهم إنه كره أخذ العطاء، فلعله رأى في وقت رده إلى من هو أحق منه أو لعله استغنى عنه أو لعله اعتقد أن فيه شبهة تمنع من أخذه وإن كان غالطاً في اعتقاده ذلك لأنه ليس بمعصوم لا يجوز الزلل عليه أو رأى أنه يستحق أكثر مما أعطاه عثمان ولم يكن يستحق عنده أكثر مما قسمه له وهذا مردود إلى اجتهاد الإمام ورأيه وليس لأحد الافتئات عليه ولا رد العطاء إذا لم يرضه أو يبين ابن مسعود أن رد العطاء لوجه يوجب فسق عثمان فينظر في ذلك فإن لم يكن معنى غير رده حمل أمره على بعض ما قلناه وكذلك ضرب عثمان إياه حمل أمره على الأليق به أولى وأما قولهم إنه منعه العطاء لسنين فإنه غير ثابت، فإن صح، فلعله كره أن يأخذه أو لعل عثمان صرفه إلى غيره لأنه كان أولى منه وهو مصيب في ذلك إذا أداه اجتهاده إليه. ومثل هذا لا يثبت بأخبار الآحاد ولا يتوصل به إلى القدح في الأئمة وفضلاء الأمة
And it was necessary that he should bring out what was in his hand and agree with him, so if he refrained from that, it is permissible for the imam to intimidate him with some beatings if ijtihad leads him to that. If the beating leads to breaking a rib, disabling a limb, or loss of sight, the imam is not sinful thereby nor liable if he intended only discipline and severity. Likewise if he accused him of oppression and sin or incited against him without intending thereby proof, and the apparent state of the imam was justice, it is permissible for him to discipline him. As for their statement that he disliked taking the stipend, perhaps he saw at the time to return it to one more deserving than him, or perhaps he dispensed with it, or perhaps he believed there was some suspicion preventing him from taking it, even if he was mistaken in that belief because he is not infallible, error is not impossible for him, or he thought he deserved more than Uthman gave him, and he did not deserve more than what he divided for him. This is referred back to the ijtihad of the imam and his opinion, and none may usurp his authority or reject the stipend if he does not approve it, unless Ibn Mas'ud made clear that rejecting the stipend for a reason necessitates calling Uthman corrupt, so that is examined. If there is no meaning other than rejection, his affair is carried upon some of what we said. Likewise, the beating of Uthman to him: carrying his affair upon what is more appropriate is better. As for their statement that he withheld the stipend for years, it is not established. If it is sound, perhaps he disliked to take it, or perhaps Uthman diverted it to another because he was more deserving, and he was correct in that if his ijtihad led him to it. Such things are not established by reports of individuals, nor is a way reached by them to disparage the imams and the virtuous of the community.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of accusations against Caliph Uthman regarding his treatment of Ibn Mas'ud. The author discusses several objections and provides plausible justifications based on ijtihad. He begins by asserting that if an imam, after proper ijtihad, uses beating for discipline, even if it causes severe injury, he is not sinful if his intent was correction, not harm. Similarly, if he accuses someone of wrongdoing or incites against them, it is allowed if his outward state is just. Then he addresses the charge that Uthman disliked taking the stipend: perhaps he deemed another more worthy, or suspected it, even if mistaken (since imams are not infallible). Alternatively, Ibn Mas'ud may have thought he deserved more. Such matters are left to the imam's ijtihad, and one cannot reject the stipend without clear proof that it implies corruption. The beating of Ibn Mas'ud is interpreted in the best possible light. The objection that Uthman withheld stipend for years is dismissed as unproven, and if true, may have been due to Ibn Mas'ud's refusal or Uthman's ijtihad. Such reports cannot be used to impugn the imams and the virtuous.

Key Terms
إمام
اجتهاد
تأديب
ضرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A caliph who causes severe injury through beatings is always sinful or tyrannical.
Correction: Baqillani argues that if the imam acts by ijtihad and intends only discipline, he is not sinful, even if the outcome is severe.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what condition makes an imam not sinful for causing severe injury from beatings?
A) If the person was guilty
B) If the imam intended only discipline and severity
C) If the beating was moderate
D) If the imam had a written order

Key Takeaway
An imam's disciplinary actions, even if severe, are not sinful if they are based on ijtihad and intended for correction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were in a position of authority and your ijtihad led you to believe that severe physical discipline was necessary, would you be able to inflict it knowing it could permanently harm someone? How would you examine your own heart to ensure your intent is purely for correction and not for ego, anger, or revenge?
?
Think of a time when you corrected someone harshly. Did you sincerely believe your action was based on sound judgment (ijtihad)? Could you have been mistaken? And even if you were right, are you willing to accept that your own severity might be blameworthy if your intention was not purely discipline but also frustration or pride?
?
This lesson teaches that the imam is not sinful if his intent is discipline, even if the outcome is severe injury. But what about the person who suffers that injury? How does this teaching sit with your sense of justice? Does it challenge you to think differently about the relationship between authority, intention, and consequence in your own life?

Defending Uthman Against Allegations
Baqillani refutes objections about stipend and beating of Ibn Mas'ud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes specific objections regarding Uthman's handling of Ibn Mas'ud's stipend and the withholding of it.

Core Idea
Multiple plausible justifications exist for Uthman's actions, preventing accusations of wrongdoing.

Having established the general principle of imam's disciplinary authority, Baqillani now turns to specific allegations against Uthman. He addresses why Ibn Mas'ud may have disliked the stipend, why Uthman might have beaten him, and the claim that Uthman withheld stipend for years. He shows that in each case, there are plausible ijtihad-based explanations that exonerate Uthman, and that such matters cannot be proven by weak reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on stipend objection
Read the section starting with 'As for their statement that he disliked taking the stipend...' and note the multiple possibilities offered.
2
List the plausible reasons for Ibn Mas'ud's rejection
Baqillani gives several reasons: returning to a worthier person, self-sufficiency, suspicion of illegitimacy (even if mistaken), or thinking he deserved more. List them.
These all exonerate Uthman.
3
Note the principle of imam's ijtihad
Baqillani says the matter is referred to the imam's ijtihad and opinion; no one may override it without clear evidence of corruption.
This is a key kalam principle.
4
Read about the beating and years objection
Baqillani briefly addresses the beating, saying it is better to interpret it in the best light. Then he tackles the claim about withholding stipend for years, dismissing it as unproven, but even if true, there are justifications.
The years objection is dismissed as unproven (ghayr thābit).
5
Understand the role of weak reports
Baqillani concludes that such reports (akhbar al-ahad) cannot be used to disparage the imams and virtuous people.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the status of the claim that Uthman withheld the stipend for years?
A) It is established and proves Uthman's injustice
B) It is not established, and even if true there are justifications
C) It is accepted because Ibn Mas'ud said so
D) It is irrelevant because stipend is not important

Key Takeaway
When faced with accusations against a caliph, one should seek plausible ijtihad-based justifications, and weak reports cannot overturn the presumption of justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a negative story about a person in authority, do you instinctively assume the worst, or do you, like Baqillani, actively seek out possible justifications? Which tendency dominates your heart, and what will you do to change it this week?
?
This lesson shows that even if Ibn Mas'ud was mistaken about the stipend's permissibility, it did not prove Uthman's injustice. Is there someone you have judged harshly based on incomplete information? Will you commit to re-examining that judgment today?
?
Baqillani dismisses unverified reports as insufficient to accuse the virtuous. How many times have you passed along a rumor or accepted a slander without confirming its source? Before the Day when your tongue will testify against you, what step will you take to guard your tongue against false accusations?

Defense of Uthman's Quran Compilation
Baqillani defends Uthman's collection and burning of Qur'anic codices as a virtue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational defense of Uthman's actions as necessary and virtuous, and how he refutes accusations of innovation and sin.

Core Idea
Uthman's Quran compilation was a duty, not an innovation.

This passage from Baqillani's Tamhid addresses a common sectarian accusation against Caliph Uthman. Critics claim his collection and burning of variant codices was an innovation and sin. Baqillani responds with three arguments rooted in necessity, precedent, and absence of prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the three objections mentioned.
The passage uses strong language: 'great ignorance' (جهل عظيم) and 'false' (باطل).
2
Identify the three objections
Look for the phrases 'As for their claim that...' (وأما تعلقهم بأن...) which introduce each objection. List them.
The objections are: 1) Uthman innovated by collecting and burning codices, 2) he preceded others, 3) it was a sin/innovation.
3
Analyze each refutation
For each objection, see how Baqillani refutes it using necessity, historical context, and absence of prohibition. Summarize each refutation in your own words.
Pay attention to the phrase 'this was obligatory upon Uthman' (وهذا كان الواجب على عثمان).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بأن عثمان جمع القرآن وحرق المصاحف وسبق إلى ذلك فإنه جهل عظيم لأن هذا من فضائله وتسديد عمله عندما حدث الاختلاف من والتهارج بين القراء وعدوان بعضهم على بعض ووجود كل ملحد ومدغل السبيل إلى الطعن في الدين وإفساد التأويل والهزل بأئمة المسلمين. وهذا كان الواجب على عثمان إذا وقع له وخطر بباله وظن الصلاح ولم الشعث؛ ولو عدل عنه لكان عاصياً مفرطاً قائداً إلى الإهمال والتضييع. وأما قولهم إنه سبق إلى ذلك فباطل لأنه قد جمع في أيام رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأيام أبي بكر وعمر في الجلود والخزف والأكتاف وغير ذلك ولم تحتج الصحابة إلى جمعه على وجه ما جمعه عثمان لأنه لم يحدث في أيامهما الخلاف بين القراء ما حدث في أيامه. وأما تعلقهم بأن جمعه معصية وبدعة فإنه جهل لأن المعصية هي ما نهي فاعلها عنها ونحن نقول إن جمعه من فرض عثمان إذا قدر في جمعه من الصلاح ما ذكرناه وليس من نص الكتاب أو السنة الثابتة أو إجماع الأمة أو حجج العقول ما يحظر جمع القرآن ويقضي على عصيان فاعله فبطل بذلك ما ظنوه
As for their claim that Uthman collected the Quran and burned the codices and preceded others in that, it is great ignorance because this is among his virtues and the correctness of his action, when the disagreement and quarrelling among the reciters and the aggression of some against others occurred, and the existence of every atheist and interloper seeking a way to attack the religion, corrupt interpretation, and mock the imams of the Muslims. This was obligatory upon Uthman when it occurred to him and crossed his mind and he thought it beneficial to gather and repair the dispersal; had he turned away from it he would have been a disobedient transgressor leading to neglect and waste. As for their statement that he preceded in that, it is false because it was collected in the days of the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) and in the days of Abu Bakr and Umar on skins, pottery, shoulder bones and other things, and the companions did not need to collect it in the manner Uthman collected it because the disagreement among the reciters that occurred in his days had not occurred in their days. As for their claim that his collection was a sin and innovation, it is ignorance because sin is that which the doer is forbidden from, and we say that his collection was a duty upon Uthman since he deemed in its collection the benefit we mentioned, and there is no text of the Book, established Sunnah, consensus of the community, or rational proofs that prohibit the collection of the Quran and necessitate the disobedience of its doer. Thus what they supposed is invalidated.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of three objections against Caliph Uthman's collection and standardization of the Quran. The author first addresses the accusation that Uthman innovated by gathering the Quran and burning differing copies. He calls this ignorance and considers it one of his virtues because it occurred when disputes among reciters and attacks on religion were rampant. Uthman was obligated to act; had he refrained, he would have been sinful. Second, the objection that he preceded others in this is false because the Quran was already compiled in the Prophet's time and under Abu Bakr and Umar, but not on a single standard because there was no dispute then. Third, the claim that this act was a sin or innovation is refuted: sin is only what is prohibited, and there is no prohibition from Quran, Sunnah, consensus, or reason against compiling the Quran. Therefore their allegation is invalid.

Key Terms
جمع القرآن
المصاحف
بدعة
معصية
إجماع الأمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman's burning of variant codices was an innovation (bid'a) and a sin rather than a necessary preservation.
Correction: Baqillani argues it was a virtuous duty, not a sin, because there is no prohibition against it and it was done to prevent greater harm.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why was Uthman's collection and burning of codices obligatory?
A) To create a new Quran.
B) To prevent disagreement among reciters and protect the religion.
C) To follow the practice of previous caliphs exactly.
D) To assert his authority as caliph.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's compilation was a praiseworthy act of preservation, not a sin or innovation, as it was a necessary duty to protect the Quran and the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of a controversial decision by a religious leader, do you instinctively judge it as innovation, or do you pause to consider the necessity and context? What would it take for you to develop the trust in scholars that the Companions had in Uthman?
?
If you were in a position of authority facing a crisis that required an unprecedented action, would you have the courage to act like Uthman, knowing you might be harshly criticized? What fears hold you back from doing what is right despite potential backlash?
?
The principle that 'sin is only what is explicitly forbidden' is liberating yet challenging. Are there actions you have avoided or condemned out of cultural habit rather than clear divine prohibition? How can you align your judgments with this principle starting today?

Defense of Uthman's Pasture Reserve
Baqillani refutes criticism of Uthman's pasture reserve as a justified measure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani defends Uthman's pasture reserve as a justified public measure, not a personal privilege.

Core Idea
Uthman's reserve prevented conflict; past caliphs did the same.

Imam Baqillani addresses a criticism leveled against Uthman ibn Affan regarding his establishment of a reserved pasture (ḥimā). Critics considered it an unjust act, but Baqillani explains the context: the charity livestock had proliferated, leading to disputes and violence. He points out that previous caliphs had also taken similar measures. This passage shows how actions of the Companions are evaluated with attention to their circumstances and precedents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the concise style of Imam Baqillani.
2
Identify the criticism and response
Find the accusation (that Uthman reserved pasture) and trace Baqillani's refutation: he calls it baseless, gives a reason, and cites precedent.
The response has three parts: dismissal, explanation, precedent.
3
Examine the precedent
Baqillani points out that Abu Bakr and Umar also reserved pastures without objection. Consider why this strengthens his case.
This shows consistency among the Rightly Guided Caliphs.
4
Reflect on the principle
The key principle is 'cutting off fitna' (ḥasm māddat al-fitna). Understand how this justifies what might otherwise seem problematic.
This is a common theme in Sunni political thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بأنه حمى الحمى، فلا حجة فيه، لأن إبل الصدقة وماشيتها كثرت واتسعت وكثرت الخصومات بين رعاة ماشية الصدقة وحفاظها وقتلوا أرباب المواشي فحسم مادة الفتنة الحمى وقد حمى أبو بكر ووسع وعمر فلم ينكر ذلك أحد ولا نقمه ولا عده من معاصيهما ؛ فلا تعلق في ذلك
And as for their claim that he reserved the pasture, there is no proof in it, because the camels of charity and their livestock increased and expanded, and disputes multiplied between the herders of charity livestock and their guardians, and they killed the owners of livestock, so he cut off the source of fitna by the reserve. And Abu Bakr reserved and expanded, and Umar, so no one denied that, nor reproached it, nor counted it among their sins. So there is no basis in that.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the accusation that Uthman's act of reserving pastureland was unjust. Baqillani explains the context: the charity livestock had increased, leading to conflicts and even killings among herders. Uthman's reserve was a measure to prevent fitna. The author then points out that previous caliphs Abu Bakr and Umar had also reserved pastures without objection. Therefore the criticism lacks merit.

Key Terms
حمى
إبل الصدقة
فتنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman's pasture reserve was a personal privilege or an unjust act.
Correction: It was a public measure to manage charity livestock and prevent conflict, with precedent from Abu Bakr and Umar.

Checkpoint

A) Because it was his personal property
B) Because it prevented conflict among herders and had precedent from Abu Bakr and Umar
C) Because the Prophet ordered it
D) Because the livestock belonged to him

Key Takeaway
Uthman's pasture reserve was justified as a measure to prevent fitna, consistent with actions of earlier caliphs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson shows that actions that appear questionable at first can have valid reasons. Think of a situation where you misjudged someone's action because you lacked context. How will you apply the lesson's lesson of seeking justification before condemnation in future?

Defense of Uthman's Exile of Abu Dharr
Baqillani defends Uthman's treatment of Abu Dharr as voluntary and legitimate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's two-part defense of Uthman's treatment of Abu Dharr and the principle that a caliph's actions are presumed just.

Core Idea
A caliph's actions are presumed just until proven otherwise.

In this passage, Baqillani tackles a historical accusation against Uthman regarding his treatment of the companion Abu Dharr. He offers two lines of defense: first, that Abu Dharr chose to leave, and second, that even if forced, Uthman's action must be presumed lawful. This reflects a key principle in Sunni theology about the integrity of the early caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Baqillani presents his argument in two parts.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify Baqillani's two defenses
Find the two distinct arguments Baqillani makes: (1) Abu Dharr chose to go, (2) even if forced, it is presumed just.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Consider why Baqillani insists on presuming justice. This connects to the belief in the 'adala (uprightness) of the Companions.
This principle is crucial in Sunni kalam.
4
Apply to a modern context
Think about how this principle of presumption of justice applies in other historical or legal contexts.
Optional reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بأنه نفى أبا ذَرٍ إلى الرَّبَذَة فباطل لأن أبا ذر اختار الخروج إليها لما خيره عثمان وكره المقام في المدينة فلا عتب على عثمان ولو صح أنه أبعده عن المدينة لم يكن بذلك مأثوماً ولوجب حمل فعله على العدل والصحة حتى يقوم دليل على ظلمه وتعديه وقد ذكر الناس أن أبا ذر كان
And as for their claim that he exiled Abu Dharr to al-Rabadha, it is false because Abu Dharr chose to go out to it when Uthman gave him the choice, and he disliked staying in Medina, so there is no blame on Uthman. And if it were established that he removed him from Medina, he would not be sinful by that, and his action must be interpreted as justice and correctness until evidence of his injustice and transgression arises. And people have mentioned that Abu Dharr was...
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the accusation that Uthman unjustly exiled Abu Dharr. Baqillani argues two points: first, Abu Dharr chose to leave when given the option, so no blame falls on Uthman. Second, even if Uthman had forcibly exiled him, the default assumption is that the action was just and lawful, unless proven otherwise. The author then begins to mention what people have said about Abu Dharr, but the text is cut off. This reflects the principle that a caliph's actions are presumed valid until contrary evidence appears.

Key Terms

عدالة الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Key Takeaway

Refuting Excuses for Completing Prayer While Traveling
Baqillani dismisses two justifications for completing prayer while traveling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani dismisses two justifications offered for Caliph Uthman completing the prayer while in Mina, and why the objections are unfounded.

Core Idea
Excuses for changing a ruling have no weight if they lack a valid connection to the law.

This passage addresses a historical objection against Caliph Uthman's action of completing the prayer (performing four rak'ahs) while in Mina during Hajj. Baqillani presents two excuses that were offered and then decisively rejects them, showing that they lack any valid basis in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the structure: two excuses followed by a dismissive question.
The final phrase 'وأي تعلق في هذا' is a rhetorical rejection.
2
Identify the two excuses
The first excuse claims Uthman considered himself resident because his family was in Makkah. The second claims he feared doubt among Arabs who had returned to their water sources.
Both excuses attempt to justify completing the prayer instead of shortening it.
3
Understand Baqillani's response
Baqillani dismisses both excuses with the rhetorical question 'what connection is there in this?' meaning they have no real basis or relevance to the ruling.
This indicates the author's view that the objection is unfounded and the excuses are invalid.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why Baqillani considers these excuses insufficient. Reflect on how kalam scholars defend the actions of the Companions when faced with objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سئل عنه أحدهما أنه قال : كان أهلي بمكة فصرت في حضر وخرجت عن حكم المسافر ; وهذا كما قال : إذا كان على أهل ومنزل ; والآخر أنه قال : بلغني أن العرب انصرفت إلى مياهها وصلت ركعتين وقالت : إن الصلاة قصرت فخفت دخول الشبهة عليهم ; وأي تعلق في هذا
He was asked about it, one of them said: 'My family was in Makkah, so I became a resident and left the ruling of the traveller.' And this is as he said: 'If he has family and a home.' And the other said: 'It reached me that the Arabs went to their waters and prayed two rak'ahs and said: The prayer is shortened, so I feared the entrance of doubt upon them.' And what connection is there in this?
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the caliph Uthman concerning his completing the prayer while in Mina during Hajj. Baqillani presents two excuses that were offered: first, that Uthman considered himself a resident because his family was in Makkah; second, that he feared that shortening the prayer would cause confusion among the Arabs who were used to praying two rak'ahs. The author then dismisses both excuses by saying 'what connection is there in this?' meaning these justifications have no real basis and do not constitute a valid objection against Uthman. The response is brief but definitive, indicating the author's view that the objection is unfounded.

شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some think Uthman's action was an innovation and that the excuses given are valid justifications.
Correction: Baqillani shows these excuses have no real connection to the legal ruling; the objection is unfounded, and Uthman's action is not incriminating.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani rejects both excuses for completing prayer while traveling, showing they have no sound basis and do not invalidate Uthman's action.

Defense of Uthman's Decision on Ubaydullah ibn Umar
Baqillani defends Uthman's decision by citing consultation, evidence, and legal discretion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the legal and political reasoning behind Uthman's decision not to execute Ubaydullah ibn Umar, as defended by Imam Baqillani.

Core Idea
Uthman's decision was based on community consultation, fear of fitna, and valid legal discretion.

Imam Baqillani addresses a historical criticism against Uthman for sparing Ubaydullah ibn Umar after he killed al-Hurmuzan, who was suspected of involvement in Umar's assassination. Baqillani argues that Uthman's action was not arbitrary but was grounded in consultation with the Companions, concern for public interest, and legal principles that allow discretionary judgment in cases of plausible guilt. This passage illustrates how early Muslim leaders balanced justice and political stability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Get a sense of Baqillani's argument before diving into details.
The translation is literal, so some phrases may seem awkward.
2
Identify the justifications
List the reasons Baqillani gives for Uthman's decision: consultation, fear of fitna, evidence against al-Hurmuzan, and legal discretion. Note how each is supported.
Look for phrases like 'عن رأي الأمة', 'فيهن الدين', and 'السعي في الأرض فساداً'.
3
Understand the legal principle
Baqillani explains that Ubaydullah acted wrongly by taking justice into his own hands, but Uthman's decision not to punish him was permissible because of the circumstances. Focus on the concept of discretionary punishment (ta'zir).
The phrase 'ليس أخذه للحق بيده حقاً' is key.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this case shows the importance of consultation and avoiding fitna in Islamic governance. Why might Uthman's decision be seen as wise rather than weak?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بتركه قتل عبيد الله بن عمر بالهرمزان، فإنه أيضاً باطل ; لأنه لم يفعل ذلك إلا عن رأي الأمة ومشورة منهم أو من أكثرهم. لأنه قيل له : أمس قُتِلَ أبوه ويُقتل اليوم ويتحدث بهذا في بلاد الكفر والإسلام، فيهن الدين ويَذلُّ سلطان المسلمين. وقيل إن الهرمزان حمل أبا لؤلؤة على قتل عمر حَمِيَّةٌ للفُرْس والمجوسية وإن إسلامه لم يكن حسناً وإنه كان يستقل فإنه كان يفرض له على ما ذُكر عشرين درهماً ويقول : إنه لا أب عطاء له في الإسلام وذكر له أن الهرمزان خرج من داره أبو لؤلؤة بالخنجر قتل عمر يُرى الخنجر تحت أثوابه فقالوا له هذا من السعي في الأرض فساداً ; وهو مستحق لما نزل به غير أن هذا إليك وإلى من يقوم بالأمر وقد تعدى عبيد الله بأخذ حقه بيده فقط وقد كان هذا منه في غير سلطانك والعقد لك وليس أخذه للحق بيده حقاً لأحد يطالب به فلا شيء الآن عليه . وقد يجوز أن يعتقد بعض الصحابة ان دم عبيد الله مستحق ولا يعتقد ذلك عثمان إذا ظن وقوي عنده أن الهرمزان سعى في الأرض فساداً بقتل عمر بن الخطاب وخاف أمثالها من التوثب على الأئمة فلا شيء عليه في ترك الله الإفادة من عبيد بن عمر
And as for their attachment to his leaving the killing of Ubaydullah ibn Umar for (killing) al-Hurmuzan, it is also false; because he did that only by the opinion of the community and consultation from them or from most of them. Because it was said to him: 'Yesterday his father was killed and today he is killed, and this will be spoken about in the lands of disbelief and Islam, disparaging the religion and humiliating the authority of the Muslims.' And it is said that al-Hurmuzan incited Abu Lu'lu'a to kill Umar out of partisanship for the Persians and Zoroastrianism, and that his Islam was not good, and that he considered (the stipend) too little, for he was allotted as mentioned twenty dirhams and said: 'There is no father of giving for me in Islam.' And it was mentioned to him that al-Hurmuzan left his house with Abu Lu'lu'a carrying the dagger that killed Umar, the dagger seen under his garments, so they said to him: 'This is spreading corruption in the land; he deserves what befell him, but this is up to you and those in charge of the affair . And Ubaydullah transgressed by taking his right by his own hand only, and this was from him without your authority and the contract is yours, and taking the right by one's own hand is not a right for anyone to claim, so there is nothing upon him now.' And it is possible that some Companions believed that Ubaydullah's blood was lawful, and Uthman did not believe that when he thought and it became strong with him that al-Hurmuzan spread corruption in the land by killing Umar ibn al-Khattab, and he feared similar acts of rebellion against the imams, so there is nothing upon him in leaving (punishment) concerning Ubaydullah ibn Umar.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Uthman's decision not to punish Ubaydullah ibn Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan, who was suspected of involvement in the assassination of Umar. Baqillani argues that Uthman did not act arbitrarily but consulted the community, who pointed out that executing Ubaydullah would cause scandal and weaken Muslim authority. Additionally, there were reports that al-Hurmuzan had conspired with Abu Lu'lu'a, the actual killer, out of Persian national pride and dissatisfaction with his stipend. Some witnesses saw al-Hurmuzan with the dagger. Thus, many considered al-Hurmuzan guilty of spreading corruption, making his killing justifiable. However, Ubaydullah acted without official authority, taking the law into his own hands, which was wrong in procedure. But since al-Hurmuzan's guilt was plausible, Uthman could exercise discretion and leave him unpunished to avoid greater fitna (civil strife). The author also notes that some Companions may have thought Ubaydullah's action was justified, while Uthman himself may have been uncertain, but his decision not to execute him was legally permissible given the circumstances.

Key Terms
الهرمزان
عبيد الله بن عمر
فتنة
مشورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim Uthman ignored justice by not punishing Ubaydullah.
Correction: Baqillani shows that Uthman acted on consultation, had plausible evidence against al-Hurmuzan, and exercised legal discretion to prevent fitna.

Checkpoint
What primary justification does Baqillani give for Uthman not killing Ubaydullah?
A) He feared Ubaydullah's tribe would rebel.
B) He consulted the community and feared causing widespread fitna.
C) He believed al-Hurmuzan was innocent.
D) He was waiting for a formal trial.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's decision was legally and politically sound due to community consultation, plausible evidence against al-Hurmuzan, and the need to prevent greater strife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been in a position to enforce a rule strictly but realized it would cause greater harm? Did you seek counsel like Uthman, or act impulsively? What would it take for you to prioritize the community's well-being over rigid justice?
?
Uthman considered the possibility that al-Hurmuzan was guilty based on strong circumstantial evidence. Think of someone you have judged negatively on incomplete evidence. How does this passage challenge you to withhold judgment until you have clearer proof? What concrete step can you take this week to be more fair in your assessments?
?
The community warned Uthman about the reputation of Islam. Are you more concerned about how your actions reflect on your faith, or about your personal comfort? If you knew that forgiving a personal right would protect Islam's honor, would you do it? Identify one right you hold against someone that you could relinquish for a greater good.

The Conditions for Valid Criticism of Uthman's Appointments
Baqillani sets conditions for a valid objection against Uthman's appointments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical prerequisites Baqillani sets for a valid objection against Uthman's appointment of his relatives.

Core Idea
Criticism requires evidence, not just accusation.

In defending Uthman, Baqillani does not simply assert the companions' righteousness; he places the burden of proof on the critics. This short passage lays out two necessary conditions for the objection to hold. Let's examine them closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'لا متعلق فيه دون أن يثبتوا...'
2
Identify the two required proofs
Baqillani says the critics must prove two things. Find what they are.
3
Reflect on the default assumption
Why does Baqillani demand this proof? What is the default position about the companions?
This reflects the principle that the companions are presumed upright unless proven otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما تعلقوا به من تولية أقاربه كمعاوية وعبد الرحمن بن عثمان ومروان بن الحكم وغيرهم، فلا متعلق فيه دون أن يُثبتوا أنهم فساق وأن فسقهم ثبت عند عثمان فأقرهم وأنه ولاهم يوم ولاهم وهم فساق ليسوا بأهل
And as for what they have clung to of his appointment of his relatives, like Mu'awiya, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Uthman, Marwan ibn al-Hakam and others, there is no ground in it unless they prove that they were sinners, and that their sinfulness was established with 'Uthman and he confirmed them, and that he appointed them on the day he appointed them while they were sinners, not being qualified.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage refutes the charge that Uthman nepotistically appointed unqualified or sinful relatives. Baqillani argues that the objection has no basis unless the critics can prove two things: first, that the appointees (such as Mu'awiya, Abd al-Rahman ibn Uthman, and Marwan) were actually sinners (fusaq); and second, that Uthman knew of their sinfulness and nevertheless confirmed them in their positions or appointed them while they were sinners and unfit. Without this proof, the accusation is unfounded. The author implies that the default assumption is that these companions were upright and qualified, and that Uthman's appointments were proper.

Key Terms
تولية
فساق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that Uthman's appointments were inherently nepotistic and blameworthy.
Correction: Baqillani argues that nepotism is not automatically wrong; it becomes blameworthy only if the appointees are unqualified or sinful, and if the appointing authority knows this.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what must critics prove for their objection against Uthman's appointments to have ground?
A) That the relatives were not from Quraysh.
B) That the relatives were sinners and Uthman knew it.
C) That the relatives were wealthy and powerful.
D) That Uthman appointed them without consultation.

Key Takeaway
A valid objection against a companion's action requires solid proof of wrongdoing and knowledge by the actor, not mere association.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you judged someone based on outward circumstances (like family connections) without knowing their inner state or the full context? How does Baqillani's condition of 'proof' challenge your natural tendency to assume the worst?
?
Think of a time you complained about a leader or a person of authority. Did you have solid evidence of wrongdoing, or were you relying on rumors or partial information? What would it look like today to apply the standard that criticism requires established proof?
?
This lesson implies that even a companion like Uthman cannot be faulted without concrete evidence. How does that change the way you listen to criticisms of Muslims, especially those in positions of leadership? Are you quick to accept accusations, or do you demand proof? Be honest: what will you do differently from today?

Defense of Uthman's Decision on Ubaydullah ibn Umar
Baqillani defends Uthman's decision not to punish Ubaydullah for killing al-Hurmuzan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's two-part defense of Uthman's refusal to punish Ubaydullah ibn Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan.

Core Idea
Uthman's inaction was justified by al-Hurmuzan's guilt and the need to protect imams.

This passage from Baqillani addresses a controversial early Islamic incident: after Umar's assassination, Ubaydullah ibn Umar killed al-Hurmuzan, a Persian who was allegedly involved. Some criticized Uthman for not punishing Ubaydullah. Baqillani explains the reasoning behind Uthman's decision, offering two key justifications that reveal the balance between justice and public interest in Islamic legal thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the two main arguments Baqillani presents: the first about al-Hurmuzan's guilt, the second about Uthman's fear of further attacks.
The first few words in Arabic are unclear due to OCR issues, but the overall meaning is discernible.
2
Understand the first argument
Baqillani notes that al-Hurmuzan was implicated in Umar's murder by allowing Abu Lu'lu'a to leave his house with a dagger. This made him a 'spreader of corruption' deserving of death, justifying Ubaydullah's act even if procedurally wrong.
3
Understand the second argument
Some Companions thought Ubaydullah's blood was licit, but Uthman disagreed. Baqillani says Uthman had strong evidence that al-Hurmuzan was complicit and feared that punishing Ubaydullah would encourage attacks on imams. This made leniency the correct choice.
4
Reflect on the principles
Consider how this case reflects the tension between strict justice and maintaining public order. Baqillani's defense shows that Uthman prioritized the stability of the community over exacting revenge.
This connects to broader discussions of maqasid and siyasa shar'iyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عمر يسمون قتل عمر حَمِيَّةٌ للفُرْس والمجوسية وإن إسلامه لم يكن حسناً وإنه كان يستقل فإنه كان يفرض له على ما ذُكر عشرين درهماً ويقول : إنه لا أب عطاء له في الإسلام وذكر له أن الهرمزان خرج من داره أبو لؤلؤة بالخنجر قتل عمر يُرى الخنجر تحت أثوابه فقالوا له هذا من السعي في الأرض فساداً ؛ وهو مستحق لما نزل به غير أن هذا إليك وإلى من يقوم بالأمر وقد تعدى عبيد الله بأخذ حقه بيده فقط وقد كان هذا منه في غير سلطانك والعقد لك وليس أخذه للحق بيده حقاً لأحد يطالب به فلا شيء الآن عليه . وقد يجوز أن يعتقد بعض الصحابة ان دم عبيد الله مستحق ولا يعتقد ذلك عثمان إذا ظن وقوي عنده أن الهرمزان سعى في الأرض فساداً بقتل عمر بن الخطاب وخاف أمثالها من التوثب على الأئمة فلا شيء عليه في ترك الله الإفادة من عبيد بن عمر
They call Umar [unclear] killing Umar out of zeal for the Persians and Magians, and that his Islam was not good, and that he was stingy, for he used to assign him twenty dirhams as mentioned, and he says: 'He has no father and no stipend in Islam.' And it was mentioned to him that al-Hurmuzan came out from his house, Abu Lu'lu'a with the dagger killed Umar, the dagger is seen under his garments. So they said to him: 'This is spreading corruption in the land; he deserves what has befallen him, but this is up to you and to those who undertake the affair. Ubaydullah has exceeded by taking his right by his hand only, and this was from him without your authority, and the contract is yours, and his taking the right by his hand is not a right for anyone to claim, so nothing is against him now.' And it may be that some Companions believed that the blood of Ubaydullah is permissible, but Uthman did not believe that, when he thought and it became strong in his view that al-Hurmuzan spread corruption in the land by killing Umar ibn al-Khattab, and he feared similar attacks on the imams, so nothing is against him in leaving [the execution] of Ubaydullah ibn Umar.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Uthman's decision regarding the case of Ubaydullah ibn Umar, who killed al-Hurmuzan after the assassination of Umar. Baqillani presents two arguments. First, he recounts the accusation that Ubaydullah acted without authority, but notes that the victim (al-Hurmuzan) was considered a spreader of corruption for his involvement in Umar's murder, thus his killing was justified. The second argument addresses the objection that some Companions thought Ubaydullah deserved execution. Baqillani explains that Uthman did not share this view because he had strong evidence that al-Hurmuzan was complicit in killing Umar and feared that punishing Ubaydullah might encourage further attacks on imams. Therefore, Uthman's inaction was justifiable.

Key Terms
الهرمزان (al-Hurmuzan)
عبيد الله بن عمر (Ubaydullah ibn Umar)
التوثب على الأئمة (attacks on imams)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman was unjustly lenient or biased towards his relatives.
Correction: Baqillani shows that Uthman acted on sound evidence of al-Hurmuzan's guilt and a legitimate fear for the safety of imams, not favoritism.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why did Uthman not punish Ubaydullah ibn Umar?
A) Because Ubaydullah was a close relative of Uthman
B) Because Uthman believed al-Hurmuzan was guilty of fasad and feared further attacks on imams
C) Because Ubaydullah had already been pardoned by Caliph Umar
D) Because the caliph had no authority over blood revenge

Key Takeaway
Uthman's decision not to punish Ubaydullah was based on al-Hurmuzan's complicity in Umar's murder and a practical concern to deter future attacks on imams.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone taking justice into their own hands, do you instinctively condemn them without considering the victim's guilt? Will you now pause to examine the evidence before judging?
?
Uthman feared that punishing Ubaydullah would embolden attacks on future leaders. Are you quick to criticize your spiritual leaders when they make unpopular decisions, without considering the wider harm to the community's stability?
?
The passage shows that even well-intentioned companions disagreed. Is your own circle of agreement so narrow that you dismiss those who differ from you? Will you commit to hearing the reasoning of those you oppose before rejecting them?
